Female Slave's Kitaba for Herself and Her Young Sons
Explanation of ruling and clarifications on female slave's kitaba for herself and sons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding a female slave's kitaba contract covering herself and her minor sons and explain why the ruling applies equally to male and female slaves.

Core Idea
A mother slave can validly contract kitaba for herself and her minor sons; one son paying frees all equally.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a key ruling from Al-Hidayah about a female slave making a kitaba (manumission contract) for herself and her two young sons. The ruling is straightforward, but three important points emerge: the scope of the contract, the equal treatment of male and female slaves, and the joint liability of the sons. You will learn why the Hanafi school affirms this contract's validity and the practical outcome when one son pays.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a female slave entering a kitaba contract on herself and her two minor sons. The ruling is that the contract is valid (sahih) and upon payment of the kitaba amount, all three become free.
2
Locate the source texts
The commentator contrasts the presentations: al-Mabsut places the issue in the context of a male slave, while al-Jami' al-Saghir places it in the context of a female slave.
3
Understand the implication
The difference removes doubt that the ruling might be limited to a father (who has guardianship). Since al-Jami' al-Saghir explicitly uses a female slave, it confirms the ruling applies equally.
4
Analyze the joint liability
The ruling is framed with two sons to illustrate that if one pays the full amount, both sons and the mother become free, and the paying son cannot seek reimbursement from the other because they are not in a principal-follower relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله واذا كاتبت الامة .... الخ ۔ مسئلہ کا حکم اور اس کا مفہوم ترجمہ سے واضح ہے مزید وضاحت کی ضرورت نہیں۔ البتہ یہاں چند امور قابل لحاظ ہیں۔ اول یه كه كاتبت الامة على نفسها بمعنى قبلت عقد الكتابة على نفسها وعلى ابنين صغيرين لھا ہے۔ دوم یہ کہ شیخ ابو جعفر نے کشف الغوامض میں ذکر کیا ہے کہ مبسوط میں اس مسئلہ کی وضع غلام کے حق میں رکھی گئی ہے اور جامع صغیر میں اس کی وضع باندی کے حق میں رکھی ہے۔ اس کا فائدہ یہ ہے کہ اگر جامع صغیر کی روایت نہ ہوتی تو مبسوط کی وضع پر یہ وہم ہوسکتا تھا کہ ممکن ہے جواز اس لئے ہو کہ باپ کو صغیر پر ولایت حاصل ہے۔ اور ماں کو یہ ولایت حاصل نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے باندی کی صورت میں جواز نہ ہوگا۔ پس جامع صغیر کی روایت سے یہ واضح ہو گیا کہ حکم مذکور غلام اور باندی دونوں کے حق میں برابر ہے۔ سوم یہ کہ مسئلہ کی وضع دو صغیر بچوں کے حق میں رکھنے سے یہ فائدہ ہوا کہ ان میں سے جو بھی عوض ادا کر دے وہ دوسرے پر رجوع نہیں کر سکتا اور وہ دونوں آزاد ہو جائیں گے۔ اگر مسئلہ کی وضع صرف ایک صغیر کے حق میں ہوتی تو اس جیسی صورت میں کوئی کہ سکتا تھا کہ اگر دو بیٹوں میں سے ایک بیٹا بدل کتابت ادا کر دے تو دوسرا بیٹا آزا انہیں نا چاہیے۔ کیونکہ ان دونوں میں نہ اصالت ہے نہ تبعیت ۔
His statement: 'And if a female slave makes kitaba...' - The ruling and meaning of the issue are clear from the translation; no further explanation is needed. However, a few points are noteworthy. First: That 'the female slave made kitaba upon herself' means she accepted the kitaba contract upon herself and upon her two young sons. Second: Sheikh Abu Ja'far mentioned in Kashf al-Ghawamid that in al-Mabsut, this issue is presented in the context of a male slave, and in al-Jami' al-Saghir, it is presented in the context of a female slave. The benefit of this is that if the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir were not present, based on the layout of al-Mabsut, one might have suspected that permissibility is because the father has guardianship over the minor, but the mother does not have this guardianship, so in the case of a female slave, it would not be permissible. Thus, through the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, it became clear that the mentioned ruling is equal in the case of both a male and a female slave. Third: That presenting the issue in terms of two young children gives the benefit that whichever of them pays the consideration cannot take recourse against the other, and both will become free. If the issue were presented only in terms of one minor, in a similar situation one could say that if one of two sons pays the kitaba price, the other son should not become free, because between the two there is neither primacy (asala) nor following (tabaiyya).
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding a female slave who makes a kitaba contract on herself and her two minor sons. The commentator notes that the ruling is clear, but highlights three important points. First, the phrase 'the female slave made kitaba upon herself' means she accepted the contract for herself and for her two young sons. Second, there is a difference in how the ruling is presented in two primary Hanafi texts: al-Mabsut places it in the context of a male slave, while al-Jami' al-Saghir places it in the context of a female slave. This is significant because it dispels any possible doubt that the ruling might be specific to a father (who has guardianship over minors) and not apply to a mother. Since al-Jami' al-Saghir explicitly deals with a female slave, it confirms the ruling applies equally to male and female slaves. Third, the ruling is framed with two minor sons to illustrate that when one son pays the full kitaba amount, both sons become free, and the paying son cannot claim reimbursement from the other. This is because they are joint principals (asil) or followers (tabi') in the contract; they are independent obligors and the payment frees both.

Key Terms
امہ (amma): A female slave.
صغیر (saghir): A minor child who has not reached puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a female slave makes a kitaba contract for herself and her two minor sons, and subsequently one of the sons pays the entire kitaba amount, what is the outcome according to the Hanafi school?
A) Only that son becomes free; the mother and other son remain enslaved.
B) All three become free, and the paying son can recover from the other son.
C) All three become free, and the paying son cannot recover from the other son.
D) The mother becomes free, but the sons remain enslaved until they also pay.

Key Takeaway
A female slave's kitaba contract covering herself and her minor sons is valid; payment by any one frees all, and no reimbursement is due between the sons.

Chapter: Kitaba of a Jointly Owned Slave - Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Divisibility
Introduces the chapter on jointly owned slave's kitaba and details the intra-Hanafi dispute on divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on the divisibility of kitaba in jointly owned slaves and apply the ruling to determine the ownership of payments.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, kitaba is divisible like emancipation according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn hold it is indivisible — the practical difference is who keeps the payments.

This lesson introduces a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the kitaba contract can be divided when a slave is jointly owned. We will examine the scenario of one co-owner authorizing the other to make kitaba of his share, and how the divergent views on divisibility affect the distribution of payments. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify the key principle (tajazzi) and its impact on rulings in muamalat.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on divisibility
Identify the view of Imam Abu Hanifa regarding whether the kitaba contract is divisible (tajazzi) when one co-owner authorizes another to make kitaba of his share.
Abu Hanifa holds that kitaba is divisible, analogous to emancipation (itq).
2
Identify the opposing view of the Sahibayn
State the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani that kitaba is not divisible, so the contract applies to the entire slave.
The Sahibayn argue that gradual freedom cannot be partial.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the evidence used by the Sahibayn to support their claim that kitaba is indivisible.
They distinguish kitaba from immediate emancipation (itq) because kitaba is a gradual process.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the objection
Understand how Abu Hanifa counters the Sahibayn's argument by analogizing kitaba to emancipation in terms of granting freedom from one aspect (min wajh).
The analogy holds despite the gradual nature; divisibility is not negated by conditionality.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Identify the practical consequence of the dispute: who owns the payments already collected when the slave becomes unable to pay.
Under Abu Hanifa, all payments go to the authorizing co-owner; under the Sahibayn, they are shared.

Chapter on Kitaba of a Jointly Owned Slave. Translation: This chapter is about the kitaba of a jointly owned slave. His statement: 'Chapter...' - According to the author of Ghayat al-Bayan, the reason for appropriateness is that non-shared ownership is the original, and shared ownership is against the original, and mentioning what is against the original after the original is appropriate. Scholars like the author of al-Inaya and other commentators have presented the justification: 'Mentioning the kitaba of two after one because one comes before two' — this justification does not apply to the first issue of the chapter according to Imam Abu Hanifa's statement. A slave is jointly owned by two men; one partner authorized the other to make kitaba of his share for one thousand dirhams and to receive the kitaba payment. He made kitaba and received some of the thousand. Then the slave became unable to pay. So the money belongs to the one who received it according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is a mukatab between them, and what he paid is between them. The basis is that kitaba is divisible in his view contrary to them, analogous to emancipation, because it grants freedom from one aspect, so it is confined to his share in his view due to divisibility. The benefit of the authorization is that he does not have the right of revocation as he would if he had not authorized. His authorizing him to receive the payment is an authorization for the slave to pay, so he [the authorizing partner] becomes a benefactor regarding his share, therefore all the received amount is for him. In their view, authorizing the kitaba of his share is an authorization to make kitaba of the whole due to lack of divisibility, so he is principal in half and agent in half, so the slave is a mukatab between them, and the received payment is common between them, so it remains common even after inability. Explanation of his statement: 'And if the slave is between two men...' A slave is jointly owned between Zaid and Amr. Zaid said to Amr: 'Make kitaba of this slave for m...'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the kitaba of a jointly owned slave. The commentator first discusses the reason for placing this chapter after the previous one, noting that the norm is individual ownership, so shared ownership comes later. However, this justification does not fit the first issue in the chapter according to Abu Hanifa's view, because that issue deals with a single slave owned by two persons, which is the opposite order. The core of the passage is the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whether the kitaba contract is divisible (tajazzi). In the scenario, one co-owner (Zaid) authorizes the other (Amr) to make kitaba of his share (nisf) for 1000 dirhams and receive the payment. Amr does so, receives part of the 1000, then the slave becomes unable to pay. According to Abu Hanifa, the kitaba is divisible like emancipation, so the contract only affects the share of the authorizing partner (Zaid). Therefore, all the money received by Amr belongs to Zaid (the authorizer). The Sahibayn hold that kitaba is not divisible; the entire slave becomes a mukatab between both owners, and the payment is shared between them. The reasoning is that kitaba is analogous to emancipation in that it grants freedom gradually, but Abu Hanifa sees it as divisible (some parts can be freed), while the Sahibayn see it as indivisible (partial freedom is impossible). The practical difference is that in Abu Hanifa's view, the authorizing partner cannot revoke the contract once given authorization, and the receiving partner (Amr) is a mere agent for collection, so the payment belongs entirely to Zaid. In the Sahibayn's view, the authorization makes Amr an agent for the whole, so the payment is joint.

Key Terms
تجزى (tajazzi): Divisibility; the legal capacity of a contract or action to apply to a part of its subject matter.
كتابة (kitaba): A contract between a master and slave whereby the slave earns money to pay for his freedom in installments.
عبد مشترك (abd mushtarak): A slave owned jointly by two or more persons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if one co-owner authorizes another to make kitaba of his share and receive payment, then the slave becomes unable to pay, to whom does the already collected payment belong?
A) It belongs entirely to the agent co-owner who collected it.
B) It belongs entirely to the authorizing co-owner.
C) It is shared equally between the two co-owners.
D) It belongs to the slave as he earned it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's dispute on the divisibility of kitaba turns on whether it is analogous to emancipation; the practical effect is who owns the payments when a jointly owned slave fails to complete his kitaba.

Children of a Mukatab: Purchased vs Born and Inheritance Rules
Intra-Hanafi dispute on purchased vs born children of a mukatab, and inheritance rules when mukatab buys his son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and Sahibayn positions on whether a purchased child is included in the kitabah, and apply the inheritance rule when the mukatab buys his own son under a single or separate kitabah.

Core Idea
The kitabah contract's terms extend only to those present at formation; purchased children are not included according to Abu Hanifa.

The status of children born to a mukatab during the kitabah period is clear — they are included and pay installments. But what about children the mukatab purchases? This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on this point, and then applies the ruling to inheritance when the mukatab buys his own son. Understanding this difference is crucial for determining whether the child can pay in installments or must pay immediately, and whether he inherits from his father.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of children
Recognize that the passage discusses 'walad mawlud' (child born during kitabah) and 'walad mushtara' (child purchased during kitabah). Note the different rulings that apply to each.
This distinction is the core of the dispute.
2
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn analogize the purchased child to the born child. They argue that both are 'tabi' (dependent) on the mukatab father, so they are included in the kitabah. The proof: the master can free the purchased child, showing his dependency.
The common illa is tabaiyyah (legal dependency).
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa distinguishes: the terms of the kitabah contract are established by stipulation and apply only to those included under the contract at formation. The purchased child was not present, so the contract's ruling does not extend to him. Hence, he must pay the entire kitabah immediately or be enslaved.
Abu Hanifa emphasizes the physical connection at contract time.
4
Apply the inheritance rule when the mukatab buys his own son
If the mukatab buys his son and then dies leaving enough property to pay the kitabah, the son inherits. This is because at the moment of death, the father is deemed free (kitabah paid), and the son's freedom is simultaneous due to dependency. The same applies if both are mukatabs under a single contract.
The condition: the son's freedom must be at the same instant as the father's.
5
Identify the exception: separate kitabah contracts
If each has a separate kitabah contract, the son does not inherit because his freedom is not retroactive — he was a slave at the father's death. This illustrates the limits of dependency.
Single contract ensures simultaneity of freedom.

Source Text

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۲ حنیفہ کے نزدیک اور صاحبین کے نزدیک ادا کرے گا اس کی میعاد پر کتابت میں پیدا شدہ فرزند پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اور امر جامع یہ ہے کہ وہ بھی مکاتب ہے میت پر تبعا اسی لئے آقا اس کو آزاد کرنے کا مالک ہوتا ہے بخلاف اس کی دیگر کمائیوں کے امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل اور وہی فرق ہے دونوں فصلوں میں یہ ہے کہ میعاد ثابت ہوتی ہے عقد میں شرط ہو کر تو یہ ہر اس کے حق میں ثابت ہوگی جو تحت العقد داخل ہوا ہو ۔ حالانکہ خریدا ہوا داخل نہیں ہوا۔ کیونکہ نہ عقد اس کی طرف مضاف ہے نہ عقد کا حکم اس تک ساری ہے اسکے الگ ہونے کی وجہ سے بخلاف کتابت میں پیدا شدہ کے کہ وہ کتابت کے وقت متصل ہے تو حکم اس تک سرایت کر گیا۔ اور جب وہ حکم کتابت میں داخل ہو تو باپ کی اقساط پر سعایت کرے گا اگر اپنے بیٹے کو خرید لیا پھر مر گیا اور بدل کے لائق مال چھوڑ گیا تو اس کا بیٹا اس کا وارث ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ جب اس کی زندگی کے آخری جزء میں اس کی آزادی کا حکم دے دیا گیا تو اسی وقت سے اس کے بیٹے کی آزادی کا بھی حکم دیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ وہ کتابت میں اپنے باپ کا تابع ہے پس یہ ایک آزاد ہوا جس نے آزاد کی میراث پائی۔ اسی طرح اگر وہ اس کا بیٹا دونوں ایک ہی کتابت میں مکاتب ہوں اس لئے کہ بیٹا اگر صغیر ہوتو وہ اپنے باپ کا تابع ہوا اور اگر بالغ ہوتو دونوں بمنزلہ ایک شخص کے قرار دئیے جائیں گے۔ پس جب باپ کی آزادی کا حکم دیا گیا تو اسی حالت میں بیٹے کی آزادی کا بھی حکم ہوگا جیسا کہ گذر چکا۔ تشریح قوله وان ترک ولداً مشترى الخ- ایک مکاتب غلام کا انتقال ہو اور اس نے وہ اولاد چھوڑی جو کتابت کی حالت میں خریدی تھی تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اولاد سے کہا جائے گا کہ یا تو فوراً کتابت ادا کر دور نہ غلام ہو جاؤ۔ صاحبین اور امام مالک کے یہاں ان کا حکم بھی اسی اولاد کا سا ہے جو کتابت کی حالت میں پیدا ہوئی ہو۔ یعنی وہ اپنے باپ کی قسطوں کے مطابق بدل کتابت ادا کریں گے۔ پس صاحبین نے ولد مشتری کو ولد مولود پر قیاس کیا ہے اور قیاس کی علت مشترکہ یہ ہے کہ دونوں تبعا مکاتب ہوتے ہیں چنانچہ خریدا ہوا فرزند بھی مکاتب میت پر بے اختیار مکاتب ہو گیا اسی لئے آقا کو یہ اختیار ہوتا ہے کہ اگر وہ چاہے تو اس کو آزاد کر دے بخلاف اس کی دیگر کمائیوں کے کہ آقا ان میں تصرف نہیں کر سکتا یہاں تک کہ اس کے خریدے ہوئے غلاموں کو آزاد نہیں کر سکتا۔
قوله ولا بي حنیفة ... الخ - امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل۔ جس سے ولد مشتری اور ولد مولود میں فرق بھی ظاہر ہو جاتا ہے یہ ہے کہ تا جبیل اس وقت ثابت ہوتی ہے جب عقد میں اس کی شرط ہو اور اسی کے حق میں ثابت ہوتی ہے جو تحت العقد داخل ہو اور خرید کردہ اولاد تحت العقد داخل نہیں کیونکہ نہ تو ان کی طرف عقد کی اضافت ہے اور نہ ان تک حکم عقد کی سرایت ہے بخلاف اس اولاد کے جو کتابت کی حالت میں پیدا ہوئی ہو کہ وہ بوقت کتابت مکاتب کے ساتھ متصل تھی اس لئے حکم عقد ان تک سرایت کر گیا۔ اس لئے وہ باپ کی قسطوں کے مطابق ادا کرے گی۔
قوله فان اشترى ابنه..... الخ - اور اگر مکاتب نے اپنے بیٹے کو خرید لیا پھر مکاتب مرگیا اور اداء بدل کتابت کے لائق مال چھوڑ گیا تو اداء بدل کتابت کے بعد باقی ماندہ مال کا وارث اس کا بیٹا ہوگا۔ اس لئے کہ جب مکاتب کی حیات کے آخری لمحات میں اس کے آزاد ہونے کا حکم دے دیا گیا تو اسی وقت سے اس کے بیٹے کے آزاد ہونے کا بھی حکم دیا جائے گا کیونکہ بیٹا کتابت میں اپنے باپ کا تابع ہے تو یہ ایسا ہوا کہ آزاد بیٹے نے آزاد باپ کی میراث پائی۔ قوله وكذلك ان كان .... الخ - اسی طرح اگر مکاتب اور اس کا بیٹا دونوں ایک ہی کتابت میں مکاتب ہوں تب بھی یہی حکم ہے اس لئے کہ بیٹا اگر صغیر ہے تو وہ اپنے باپ کا تابع ہوا اور اگر وہ بالغ ہے تو وہ دونوں بمنزلہ شخص واحد قرار دیے جائیں گے اس لئے کہ جب کتابت بھی واحدہ ہے اور اس کی قسطیں بھی واحدہ ہیں تو اگر وہ بدل کتابت ادا کر دیں تو دونوں ایک ساتھ آزاد ہوں گے اور اگر بدل کتابت ادا نہیں کرتے تو دونوں غلامی کی طرف لوٹا دیئے جائیں گے اور باپ چونکہ اپنی حیات کے آخری لمحات میں آزاد قرار پاچکا ہے اس لئے اسی وقت سے اس کے بیٹے کی آزادی کا بھی حکم دیا جائے گا پھر کتابت واحدہ کی قید اس لئے ہے کہ اگر ان میں سے ہر ایک علیحدہ عقد سے مکاتب ہو اور فرزند اپنے باپ کی موت کے بعد بدل کتابت ادا کرے تو بیٹا باپ کا وارث نہ ہوگا جیسا کہ شیخ محبوبی نے ذکر کیا ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں بیٹا مقصود بالکتابہ ہے اور اس کی آزادی بالاستناد۔ اداء بدل کے وقت سے ہوگی اس لئے کہ استناد تو ضرورت کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے اور یہاں اسکے حق میں کوئی ضرورت نہیں۔ اور جب اس کی آزادی مستند نہ ہوئی تو وہ اپنے باپ کی موت کے وقت غلام تھا۔ فلهذا لا يرثه
According to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, the purchased child will pay based on the term of the kitabah, analogized to the child born during kitabah, with the common factor that he is also a mukatab dependently. Therefore, the master has the right to free him, unlike other earnings. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa, which shows the distinction between the two types, is that the term is established by stipulation in the contract, and it will be established for everyone who is included under the contract, whereas the purchased one was not included. Because neither is the contract directed towards him nor does the ruling of the contract extend to him due to his separateness, unlike the child born during kitabah, who was connected at the time of kitabah, so the ruling extended to him. And when he is included in the kitabah ruling, he will work for the father's installments. If he bought his son and then died leaving enough money to pay the kitabah, his son will inherit him. Because when the ruling of his freedom was given in the last part of his life, at that same moment the ruling of his son's freedom is also given because he is dependent on his father in the kitabah. So this is a free person who inherited from a free person. Similarly, if both are mukatabs in the same kitabah, because if the son is a minor, he is dependent on his father, and if he is an adult, both are considered like one person. So when the father's freedom is ruled, in that same state the son's freedom is also ruled as mentioned. Explanation: The saying 'And if he leaves a purchased child' - A mukatab slave dies and leaves children who were purchased during the kitabah. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the children are told either to pay the kitabah immediately or become slaves. According to the Sahibayn and Imam Malik, their ruling is the same as that of children born during kitabah: they will pay the kitabah according to the father's installments. So the Sahibayn analogized the purchased child to the born child, and the common cause (illa) is that both are mukatabs dependently. Thus the purchased child also became a mukatab involuntarily due to the deceased mukatab, and for that reason the master has the option to free him, unlike other earnings. The saying 'And there is for Abu Hanifa...' - The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa, which also shows the distinction between the purchased child and the born child, is that the term is established when it is stipulated in the contract, and it is established for those who are included under the contract. The purchased children were not included under the contract because neither is the contract directed towards them nor does the ruling of the contract extend to them, unlike children born during kitabah, who were connected with the mukatab at the time of kitabah, so the ruling of the contract extended to them. Therefore, they will pay according to the father's installments. The saying 'If he bought his son...' - And if the mukatab bought his son and then the mukatab died leaving property sufficient to pay the kitabah, then after paying the kitabah, the son will inherit the remaining property. Because when the ruling of his freedom was given in the last moments of the mukatab's life, at that same moment the ruling of his son's freedom is also given, because the son is dependent on his father in the kitabah. So it is as if a free son inherited from a free father. The saying 'And similarly if they were...' - Similarly, if the mukatab and his son are both mukatabs in the same kitabah, the same ruling applies. Because if the son is a minor, he is dependent on his father, and if he is adult, both are considered like one person. Because when the kitabah is single and its installments are single, if they pay the kitabah, both become free together, and if they do not pay, both are returned to slavery. And since the father is already ruled free in the last moments of his life, from that same moment the son's freedom is also ruled. The condition of a single kitabah is because if each of them is mukatab by a separate contract and the son pays the kitabah after his father's death, the son will not inherit the father, as mentioned by Shaykh Mahbubi. Because in that case the son is intended by the kitabah and his freedom is by istinad (retroactivity) from the time of payment, because istinad occurs due to necessity, and here there is no necessity in his case. And when his freedom is not retroactive, he was a slave at the time of his father's death, therefore he does not inherit him.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules regarding the children of a mukatab (a slave who has contracted kitabah with his master). There are two types of children: those born during the kitabah (walad mawlud) and those purchased by the mukatab (walad mushtara). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) analogize the purchased child to the born child, arguing that both are dependent on the mukatab father and thus are included in the kitabah contract. They must pay the installments according to the father's schedule. Imam Abu Hanifa disagrees, distinguishing that the purchased child was not present at the time of the contract; the contract's terms and its ruling do not extend to him. Therefore, the purchased child must either pay the entire kitabah immediately or be returned to slavery. The text then covers a specific case: if the mukatab buys his own son and then dies leaving enough property to cover the kitabah, the son inherits from him. This is because at the moment of death, the father is deemed free (as the kitabah is paid), and the son's freedom is also established simultaneously since he is dependent on the father in the kitabah. The same applies if both father and son are mukatabs under a single kitabah contract. However, if each has a separate kitabah contract, the son's freedom is not retroactive; he was a slave at the father's death and thus does not inherit. This lesson clarifies the intra-Hanafi difference and the practical implications for inheritance and the status of children.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukātab): A slave who has made a contract of kitabah with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
کتابت (kitābah): The contract between a master and slave for the slave's freedom in exchange for specified payments.
ولد مولود (walad mawlūd): A child born to a mukatab during the period of kitabah.
ولد مشتری (walad mushtarā): A child purchased by a mukatab during the kitabah.
تبعیت (taba‘iyyah): Legal dependency or subordination; the status of being subsidiary to another person in a legal matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is a child purchased by a mukatab treated like a child born during the kitabah?
A) Because the purchased child was present at the contract formation.
B) Because both children are legally dependent (tabi') on the mukatab father.
C) Because the master cannot free the purchased child.
D) Because the contract's terms apply to all acquisitions.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the kitabah contract's coverage is determined at formation; purchased children are not automatically included (Abu Hanifa), unlike born children, and inheritance depends on single versus separate contracts.

Kitaba Continues After Master's Death
States ruling that master's death does not void kitaba and heirs take over

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the death of a mukatab's master and explain why the kitaba is not voided, including the positions on emancipation by heirs.

Core Idea
The mukatab's right to freedom survives the master's death; the kitaba contract continues with the heirs.

When the master of a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) dies, one might assume the kitaba contract ends. However, Hanafi fiqh protects the mukatab's acquired right to freedom. In this lesson, we examine the ruling that the kitaba is not voided, how the heirs step into the master's role, and the conditions under which the heirs can emancipate the mukatab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the basic ruling: the kitaba contract is not voided upon the master's death. This is the central legal point.
The ruling is a default principle; death does not terminate the contract.
2
Understand the Rationale
Explain why: to prevent nullifying the mukatab's right to freedom. The kitaba is a cause of freedom, and a person's right must be preserved.
The rationale relies on the principle that rights once established are not easily lost.
3
Succession of Heirs
Describe how the heirs succeed the master in collecting the installments according to the original schedule. The contract terms remain unchanged.
The heirs do not own the mukatab individually but inherit the right to receive payments.
4
Emancipation by Heirs
Distinguish between one heir freeing the mukatab (invalid) and all heirs freeing collectively (valid, treated as remission of the remaining debt).
Partial ownership prevents a single heir from effecting emancipation or remission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مکاتب کا آقا فوت ہو جائے تو کتابت فسخ نہیں ہوگی تا کہ مکاتب کے حق کا ابطال لازم نہ آئے
سبا
قال و اذا مات مولى المكاتب لم تنفسخ الكتابة كيلا يؤدى الى ابطال حق المكاتب اذ الكتابة الحرية وسبب حق المرء حقه وقيل له اذا المال الى ورثة المولى على نجومه لانه استحق الحرية على هذا الوجه والسبب انعقد كذلك فيبقى بهذه الصفة ولا يتغير الا ان الورثة يخلفونه فى الاستيفاء فان اعتقه احد الورثة لم ينفذ عتقه لانه لم يملكه وهذا لان المكاتب لا يملک بسائر اسباب الملك فكذا بسبب الوراثة فان اعتقوه جميعا عتق وسقط عنه بدل الكتابة لانه يصير ابراءً عن بدل الكتابة فانه حقهم وقد جرى فيه الارث فاذا بـرئـي المكاتب عن بدل الكتابة يعتق كما اذا ابرأه المولى الا انه اذا اعتقه احد الورثة لا يصير ابراء عن نصيبه لانا نجعله ابراء اقتضاء تصحيحاً لعتقه والاعتاق لا يثبت بابراء البعض او ادائه في المكاتب لا في بعضه ولا فى كله ولا وجه الى ابراء الكل لحق بقية الورثة والله اعلم
ترجمہ اگر مر جائے مکاتب کا آقا تو کتابت فسخ نہ ہوگی ۔ تاکہ یہ حق مکاتب کے ابطال کی طرف نہ پہنچا دے اس لئے کہ کتابت
If the master of the mukatab dies, the kitaba is not voided, so that it does not lead to nullifying the right of the mukatab, for kitaba is [a contract for] freedom and a cause of a person's right is his right. It is said to him: the property [goes] to the heirs of the master upon his installments, because he deserves freedom in this manner, and the cause was so contracted, so it remains in this quality and does not change, except that the heirs succeed him in collection. If one of the heirs frees him, his emancipation is not effective because he did not own him, and this is because the mukatab is not owned by other means of ownership, thus not by inheritance. If all of them free him, he is freed and the kitaba payment is dropped, because it becomes a remission of the kitaba payment, for it is their right and inheritance has taken place in it. So if the mukatab is released from the kitaba payment, he is freed as if the master had remitted it. Except that if one heir frees him, it does not become a remission of his share, because we would make it a remission by implication to validate his emancipation, and emancipation is not established by a partial remission or payment in the mukatab, neither in part nor in whole, and there is no way to a full remission because of the right of the other heirs. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about the effect of the master's death on the kitaba contract. The ruling: the kitaba is not voided. The reason is to prevent nullification of the mukatab's right to freedom, because the kitaba is a cause of freedom and a person's right is preserved. The kitaba continues with the master's heirs, who stand in his place for collecting the installments. The mukatab's payments go to the heirs in the same schedule. If one of the heirs attempts to free the mukatab individually, that emancipation is not valid because that heir does not own the mukatab exclusively; the mukatab is not owned by inheritance like other property. However, if all the heirs collectively free the mukatab, it is effective, and the remaining kitaba debt is dropped, considered as remission (ibra') from the heirs. But a single heir cannot force a partial remission because emancipation cannot be partial regarding a mukatab. The passage emphasizes that the kitaba contract is a right of the mukatab that survives the master's death.

Key Terms
ورثة (warathah): The heirs of the deceased master who inherit the right to collect kitaba installments.
ابراء (ibra'): Remission of debt; here, when all heirs free the mukatab, the kitaba payment is considered remitted.
عتق (itq): Emancipation; freeing a slave. In context, valid only when all heirs agree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, what happens to the kitaba contract when the master dies?
A) It is voided.
B) It continues with the heirs who collect installments.
C) The mukatab is immediately free.
D) The mukatab becomes a regular slave.

Key Takeaway
The death of a mukatab's master does not void the kitaba; the mukatab's right to freedom is preserved, and the heirs step into the master's role, but individual emancipation is invalid without collective agreement.

Wala' of Children from a Freedwoman and a Non-Arab Husband
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wala' of children of freedwoman and non-Arab husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on wala' of children of a freedwoman married to a non-Arab and identify the mufta bihi position with its supporting reasoning.

Core Idea
When a freedwoman marries a non-Arab, the strength of wala' overcomes weak lineage—children's wala' belongs to the mother's patrons.

Wala' is a legal relationship that carries inheritance and blood-money liability. When a freedwoman (mu'taqah) of Arab origin marries a non-Arab (ajami) and has children, a dispute arises among the Hanafi imams: should the children's wala' follow the mother's patrons or the father's lineage? The answer turns on the relative strength of wala' versus lineage among non-Arabs. This lesson unpacks the arguments and the school's preferred view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi consensus position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the wala' of the children belongs to the mother's patrons (mawali al-umm). This is the relied-upon view in the school.
This is the default position in Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the opposing view (Abu Yusuf)
Abu Yusuf holds that the children follow the father's lineage and status, so no wala' attaches to them; they are free and their inheritance goes through dhawi al-arham.
Abu Yusuf analogizes to the case of an Arab father, where lineage is strong.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (lahuma)
The Hanafi proof is that wala' of emancipation is strong (qawi) and considered in legal rulings like kafa'ah. Non-Arab lineage is weak (da'if) because they neglect it, so it cannot oppose wala'.
This is a rational argument (istihsan) based on the principle that the strong prevails over the weak.
4
Examine Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf argues that lineage is to the father (al-nasab ila al-ab), as in the Arab case. The father is free, so children are free and cannot bear wala'.
He excludes the case where the father is a slave, as that father is 'effectively dead'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and thamarah al-khilaf
The school adopts Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's view. The practical difference: under their view, the mother's patrons inherit from the child and are liable for 'aqlah; under Abu Yusuf's view, the child has no wala' and inherits through distant relatives (dhawi al-arham).
The later Hanafi masters (e.g., al-Marghinani) rely on the Zahir al-Riwayah position.

Source Text

قال ومن تزوج من العجم بمعتقة من العرب فولدت له اولادا فولاء اولادها لمواليها عند ابي حنيفة قال رضى الله عنه وهو قول محمد وقال ابويوسف حكمه حكم ابيه لان النسب الى الأب كما اذا كان الاب عربيا بخلاف ما اذا كان الاب عبدا لانه هالك معنى ولهما ان ولاء العتاقة قوى معتبر في حق الاحكام حتى اعتبرت الكفاءة فيه والنسب فى حق العجم فانهم ضيّعوا انسابهم ولهذا لم تعتبر الكفاءة فيما بينهم بالنسب والقوى لا يعارضه الضعيف بخلاف ما اذا كان الاب عربيا لان انساب العرب قوية معتبرة في حكم الكفاءة والعقل لما ان تناصرهم بها فاغنت عن الولاء قال رضى الله عنه الخلاف في مطلق المعتقة والوضع في معتقة العرب وقع اتفاقا
He said: Whoever among the non-Arabs marries a freedwoman of the Arabs, and she bears him children, then the wala' of her children belongs to her patrons according to Abu Hanifa - may Allah be pleased with him - and he said this is also the saying of Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: His ruling is the ruling of his father, because lineage is to the father, just as if the father were an Arab, unlike if the father were a slave, for he is effectively dead. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the wala' of emancipation is strong, considered in the matter of rulings, even to the extent that compatibility is considered in it, while lineage among the non-Arabs is weak because they have neglected their lineages, and therefore compatibility is not considered among them based on lineage, and the strong is not opposed by the weak. Unlike if the father were an Arab, because the lineages of the Arabs are strong, considered in the ruling of compatibility and blood wit, because they support each other through it, and thus it suffices from wala'. He (the author) said - may Allah be pleased with him - the disagreement is in absolute terms regarding the freedwoman, but the setting of the issue regarding a freedwoman of the Arabs is by agreement.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the wala' (clientage) of children born to a freedwoman (mu'taqah) who is married to a non-Arab (ajami). The matn from al-Hidayah states the ruling: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, the wala' of the children belongs to the patrons (mawali) of the mother, not the father. Abu Yusuf differs, holding that the children follow the father's status, so they would be free and their wala' is not established. The reasoning: Abu Yusuf analogizes to the case where the father is Arab, where children follow the father's lineage. He excludes the case where the father is a slave, who is 'effectively dead'. The response from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (termed 'lahuma' in the text) argues that wala' of emancipation is strong and is considered in legal rulings such as compatibility (kafa'ah) in marriage. Among non-Arabs, lineage is weak and neglected, so it cannot oppose the strong wala'. If the father were Arab, his lineage is strong, so it would overpower wala'. The text notes that the disagreement is general, but the specific setting of an Arab freedwoman is agreed upon—that the children's wala' goes to her patrons. This ruling is important in inheritance and blood money liability.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala'): Clientage relationship arising from emancipation, entailing inheritance rights and blood money liability.
معتقة (mu'taqah): A female slave who has been emancipated.
كفاءة (kafa'ah): Compatibility in marriage, considered in lineage, profession, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if a non-Arab (ajami) marries a freedwoman of the Arabs, to whom does the wala' of their children belong?
A) The father's patrons
B) The mother's patrons
C) The father's tribe
D) No wala' attaches

Key Takeaway
The strength of wala' over weak non-Arab lineage determines that children of a freedwoman inherit wala' through their mother's patrons.

Wala' of a Child from a Free Non-Arab Father and a Freedwoman Mother
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wala' of child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the Hanafi imams on the wala' of a child from a free non-Arab father and a freedwoman mother, and explain why the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Wala' al-itaqah is stronger than wala' al-muwalat, so even a free non-Arab father's weak lineage cannot overcome the mother's strong wala'.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about patronage rights when a free non-Arab man marries a freedwoman. The central question is: whose patrons gain wala' over the child? The answer affects inheritance and illustrates the Hanafi hierarchy of patronage types, distinguishing between strong wala' al-itaqah and weak wala' al-muwalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the dispute
Note the father is a free non-Arab (ajami) who made a muwalat contract, and the mother is a freedwoman (mu'taqah). The dispute is over the child's wala'.
The father's freedom and non-Arab lineage are key.
2
Understand the two types of wala'
Wala' al-itaqah (patronage by manumission) is strong and cannot be annulled. Wala' al-muwalat (patronage by contract) is weak and can be annulled. This distinction drives the arguments.
3
Follow the argument of tarfain (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
They argue that the mother's wala' al-itaqah is strong, so the child's wala' goes to her patrons. The father's weak wala' al-muwalat cannot overcome the strong wala'.
The child's lineage through a non-Arab father is considered weak, so it does not compete.
4
Follow the argument of Abu Yusuf
He analogizes wala' to lineage, which follows the father. Therefore the child's wala' should follow the father's patrons, or if the father has none (since he is free non-Arab), the child has no wala' and inheritance goes to dhawi al-arham.
Abu Yusuf and the three Imams (including al-Shafi'i) hold this view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The tarfain position is mufta bihi because wala' al-itaqah is stronger. Practically, the child's inheritance goes to the mother's patrons, not the father's.
This affects estate distribution and highlights the hierarchy of wala'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ اختلاف مطلق معتقہ کے بارے میں ہے۔ اور معتقہ عرب کے بارے میں مسئلہ کی وضع صرف اتفاقی طور پر ہے۔ جامع صغیر میں ہے کہ ایک نبطی کا فرنے کسی کی معتقہ سے نکاح کیا پھر نبی مسلمان ہو گیا۔ اور اس نے کسی سے موالات کر لی اس کے بعد مذکورہ بیوی سے اولاد ہوئی تو طرفین فرماتے ہیں کہ اولاد کے موالی ان کی ماں کے موالی ہوں گے اور امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ ان کے موالی باپ کے موالی ہوں گے ۔ کیونکہ ولا و اگر چہ کمزور ہے تا ہم باپ کی جانب سے ہے تو ایک آزاد جمی اور ایک آزاد عر بیہ سے ہونے والی اولاد کی طرح ہو گیا۔ طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ولاء موالات کمزور ہے یہاں تک کہ نسخ کو قبول کرتی ہے۔ اور ولاء عتاقہ فتح کو قبول نہیں کرتی اور قوی کے مقابلہ میں ضعیف ظاہر نہیں ہوتا۔ اور اگر ماں اور باپ دونوں آزاد کردہ ہوں تو نسبت باپ کی قوم کی جانب ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ آزادی میں وہ دونوں برابر ہیں۔ اور باپ کی جانب کو ترجیح ہے۔ ولاء کے مشابہ نسب ہونے کی وجہ سے یا اس لئے کہ قوم اب سے نصرت زیادہ پہنچتی ہے۔
تشريح قوله ومن تزوج من العجم.. الخ - ایک حر الاصل عمر نے ایک عورت سے شادی کی جو کسی کی آزاد کی ہوئی تھی (خواہ وہ عورت عجمیہ ہو یا عربیہ ہو۔ نیز اس کا آزاد کرنے والا کوئی عربی ہو یا عجمی ہو ) اس سے کوئی اولاد ہوئی تو اولاد کی ولاء اسی معتقہ عورت کے موالی کو ملے گی گو اس کے عجمی شوہر نے کسی سے عقد موالاۃ کر لیا ہو۔ یہ حکم طرفین کے نزدیک ہے۔
امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک اس اولاد کا حکم اس کے باپ کا ہے۔ یعنی وہ آزاد ہوگی۔ پس اس پر ولاء عقاقہ نہ ہوگی ۔ بلکہ اس کا مال اس کے ذوی الارحام کے لئے وراثت ہو گا ائمہ ثلاثہ بھی اسی کے قائل ہیں، کیونکہ ولاء بمنزلہ نسب کے ہے اور نسب آباء کی جانب ہوتا ہے۔ چنانچہ باپ اگر عربی ہو تو اولاد کا بھی یہی حکم تھا۔ بخلاف اس کے جب باپ غلام ہو کہ وہ بمنزلہ مردہ کے ہے اس لئے اولاد کی ولاء اس کی ماں کے موالی کو ملتی ہے۔
The author of Hidayah says: This dispute is about an absolute freedwoman. And the placement of the issue about an Arab freedwoman is only coincidental. In al-Jami' al-Saghir it is: A Nabati unbeliever married someone's freedwoman, then the Nabati became Muslim and made a contract of muwalat with someone. Thereafter children were born from the mentioned wife. Then the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say: The children's patrons are their mother's patrons. Imam Abu Yusuf says: Their patrons are their father's patrons, because wala' although weak, is from the father's side, so it becomes like the child of a free non-Arab and a free Arab woman. The two's proof is that wala' muwalat is weak, to the point that it accepts annulment, whereas wala' al-itaqah does not accept annulment, and the weak does not appear against the strong. If both mother and father are freed, then the lineage is to the father's tribe, because they are equal in freedom, and precedence is given to the father's side, either because wala' resembles lineage, or because support comes more from the father's side. Explanation of his statement: 'And whoever marries from the non-Arabs...' etc. - A free-born 'Ajam man married a woman who was someone's freedwoman (whether that woman is non-Arab or Arab, and whether her manumitter is Arab or non-Arab). Children were born from this. Then the children's wala' goes to the patrons of this freedwoman, even if her non-Arab husband made a contract of muwalat with someone. This is the ruling according to the two. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the ruling for this child is like that of his father; meaning he will be free. Hence wala' al-itaqah will not apply to him. Rather his wealth will be inherited by his relatives (dhawi al-arham). The three Imams also hold this view. Because wala' is like lineage, and lineage goes to the father. Thus if the father was Arab, the same ruling applied for the child. Contrary to when the father is a slave, for he is like a dead person, so the child's wala' goes to the mother's patrons.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the wala' (patronage) of a child born from a free non-Arab (ajami) father and a freedwoman (mu'taqah) mother. The Hidayah commentator presents two positions. The first is that of Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Hanifa (referred to as 'tarfain' or 'the two'): the child's wala' belongs to the mother's patrons (muwalis) because wala' al-itaqah (patronage by manumission) is stronger than wala' al-muwalat (patronage by contract). The father's wala' is weak if he is non-Arab, as non-Arabs have weak lineage. The second position is that of Imam Abu Yusuf: the child's wala' follows the father, because wala' is analogous to lineage, and lineage is traced through the father. Abu Yusuf analogizes to a free Arab father, where the child's lineage and wala' would be from the father. The passage also notes that if both parents are freed slaves (ma'tuqayn), the child's lineage is to the father's tribe, either due to resemblance to lineage or because support from the father's side is greater. The practical consequence of this dispute affects inheritance: if the child's wala' goes to the mother's patrons, those patrons inherit from the child; if to the father's patrons (or none) then inheritance goes to the child's maternal relatives (dhawi al-arham). The mufta bihi (relied upon) position in the Hanafi school is that of Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifa, as indicated by the text's emphasis on the strength of wala' al-itaqah.

Key Terms
ولاء عقاقہ (wala' al-itaqah): Patronage by manumission, the bond between a master and his freed slave.
ولاء موالات (wala' al-muwalat): Patronage by contract, where a non-Arab Muslim enters into a patronage agreement with an Arab tribe for mutual support.
معتقہ (mu'taqah): A freedwoman, a female slave who has been manumitted.
عجمی (ajami): A non-Arab, specifically here one without recognized lineage ties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, to whom does the wala' of a child belong when the father is a free non-Arab and the mother is a freedwoman (mu'taqah)?
A) The child's wala' goes to the father's patrons because lineage follows the father.
B) The child's wala' goes to the mother's patrons because wala' al-itaqah is stronger.
C) The child has no wala' and inheritance goes to dhawi al-arham.
D) The child's wala' goes to the mother if the father was formerly a slave.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school relies on the strength of wala' al-itaqah: when a free non-Arab father's lineage is weak, the mother's manumission-based wala' prevails, and the child's patronage goes to her patrons.

Wala' of Child When Father Makes Muwalat After Conversion
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wala' of child from muwalat father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the tarfain (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rule that the child's wala' belongs to the mother's patrons, and evaluate Abu Yusuf's counter-argument.

Core Idea
Wala' al-muwalat is weak and annullable; wala' al-itaqah is strong — the weak yields to the strong.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute about the wala' of a child born to a non-Arab father who converted to Islam and made a muwalat contract, and a freedwoman mother. The central question is whether the father's newly acquired wala' through muwalat can override the mother's stronger wala' through emancipation ('itaqah). We will follow the reasoning of the tarfain (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Imam Abu Yusuf, and understand why the tarfain's view is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
Read the passage carefully and identify the key elements: a despicable non-Arab non-believer (Nabati) marries a freedwoman (freed by a tribe), then he converts to Islam and makes a contract of muwalat with another tribe. They have a child. The mother is a Christian or Jewess (and thus her wala' belongs to her freed patrons).
Note that the father has no lineage ('asabah) because he is a non-Arab; his only connection to a tribe is through the weak muwalat contract.
2
State the tarfain position and their proof
Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the tarfain) rule that the child's patrons are the mother's patrons (i.e., the tribe that freed her). Their proof: wala' al-muwalat is weaker than wala' al-itaqah; it can be annulled, while wala' al-itaqah is permanent. The weak does not prevail against the strong.
This is a straightforward application of strength hierarchy.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his analogy
Imam Abu Yusuf argues that the child's patrons are the father's patrons (the tribe with whom he made muwalat). He analogizes to the case of a free non-Arab father and a free Arab mother, where lineage (nasab) goes to the father by consensus. He reasons that even though muwalat wala' is weak, it still exists, and thus the child should take the father's wala' just as lineage goes to the father.
Abu Yusuf emphasizes the existence of some wala' from the father's side, however weak.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
The tarfain respond by reaffirming that weak wala' cannot counter strong wala'. The analogy to a free Arab father is invalid because Arab lineage is strong, whereas a non-Arab has no strong lineage. The fundamental principle is that when two wala' claims conflict, the stronger prevails — here the mother's wala' al-itaqah is far stronger.
This response highlights the different treatment of Arab vs. non-Arab in wala' matters.
5
Note the additional unanimous case
The passage adds that if both parents are freed slaves (ma'tuqayn), the child's lineage (and thus wala') goes to the father's tribe by consensus. This is because both parents have equal wala' status, and the father is preferred due to resemblance of wala' to lineage or greater support from the father's side.
This case does not involve muwalat; it reinforces the hierarchy when both have the same type of wala'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وفي الجامع الصغير .. الخ - امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں معتقہ کو مطلق ذکر کیا ہے۔ نیز جامع صغیر کی عبارت ولاء موالات پر بھی مشتمل ہے۔ اسلئے صاحب ہدایہ جامع صغیر کی عبارت ذکر کر رہے ہیں ۔ فرماتے ہیں کہ جامع صغیر میں ہے کہ ایک مبلی (رزیل) کافر نے کسی قوم کی آزاد کی ہوئی عورت سے نکاح کیا ، پھر وہ بھی مسلمان ہو گیا اور اس نے کسی سے موالات کرلی ۔ اس کے بعد اس کی مذکورہ بیوی سے ( جو نصرانیہ یا یہودیہ ہے ) اولاد پیدا ہوئی تو طرفین کے نزدیک اس اولاد کے موالی اس کی ماں کے موالی ہوں گے اور امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک ان کے موالی باپ کے موالی ہوں گے ۔ اس لئے کہ ولاء اگر چہ کمزور ہے تا ہم باپ کی جانب سے موجود ہے۔ تو ایسا ہو گیا۔ جیسے ایک آزاد جمی اور ایک آزاد عر بیہ کے مابین پیدا ہونے والی اولاد کہ اس صورت میں نسب بالا تفاق باپ کی جانب ہوتا ہے ایسے ہی یہاں ہوگا۔ ولهما ان ولاء المولاة الخ - طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ولاء موالات ولاء عقاقہ کی بہ نسبت ضعیف وکمزور ہے یہاں تک کہ وہ فسخ ہو سکتی ہے اور ولاء عتاقہ فسخ نہیں ہو سکتی ۔ اور یہ بات طے شدہ ہے کہ قومی کے مقابلہ میں ضعیف کا ظہور نہیں ہوتا ۔ اور اگر مذکور د اولاد کے ماں باپ دونوں آزاد کردہ ہوں تو نسبت بالا تفاق باپ کی قوم کی جانب ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ آزاد ہونے میں وہ دونوں برابر ہیں اور باپ کی جانب کو تر جیح یا تو اس لئے ہے کہ ولا ء کو نسب سے مشابہت ہے یا اس لئے کہ باپ والوں سے نصرت زیادہ پہنچتی ہے۔
His statement: 'And in al-Jami' al-Saghir...' etc. - Imam Muhammad mentioned the freedwoman absolutely in al-Jami' al-Saghir. Also the wording of al-Jami' al-Saghir includes wala' al-muwalat. Therefore the author of Hidayah mentions the wording of al-Jami' al-Saghir. He says: In al-Jami' al-Saghir it is: A despicable non-believer (Nabati) married a woman who was freed by some people. Then he also became Muslim and made a contract of muwalat with someone. Thereafter a child was born from the mentioned wife (who is a Christian or Jewess). Then according to the two (Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), the child's patrons are his mother's patrons. And according to Imam Abu Yusuf, the child's patrons are his father's patrons, because wala' although weak, exists from the father's side. So it became like a child born between a free non-Arab and a free Arab woman, in which case lineage unanimously goes to the father. Similarly here. And for the two: wala' al-muwalat... - The proof of the two is that wala' al-muwalat is weaker than wala' al-itaqah, to the extent that it can be annulled, whereas wala' al-itaqah cannot be annulled. And it is established that the weak does not appear against the strong. And if both parents of the said child are freed (ma'tuqayn), then lineage unanimously goes to the father's tribe. Because in being freed they are equal, and the father's side is preferred either because wala' resembles lineage, or because support from the father's side is greater.

Commentary
This passage continues the intra-Hanafi dispute but adds the element of the father converting to Islam and making a muwalat contract. The scenario involves a despicable non-Arab non-Muslim who marries a freedwoman, then converts and makes a muwalat contract with a tribe. They have a child. The tarfain (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) again rule the child's wala' goes to the mother's patrons because wala' al-itaqah is stronger. Though the father now has wala' via muwalat, it is weak and can be annulled. Imam Abu Yusuf analogizes to a free Arab father, where lineage goes to the father, so here too the child's wala' should go to the father's patrons. The tarfain respond by emphasizing the weakness of muwalat compared to itaqah. Additionally, when both parents are freed slaves, the child's lineage goes to the father's tribe unanimously, because both are equal in freedom status and the father's side is preferred due to resemblance to lineage or greater support. Key Terms
موالات (muwalat): A contract of patronage between a non-Arab Muslim and an Arab tribe for mutual support and inheritance.
نسب (nasab): Lineage or genealogy.

B) The child's wala' goes to the mother's patrons because wala' al-itaqah is stronger.
C) The child has no wala' and his inheritance goes to the public treasury.
D) The child's wala' goes to the father's patrons only if the mother is also a freedwoman.

Key Takeaway
The strength of wala' determines the child's patrons: strong wala' al-itaqah overrides weak wala' al-muwalat.

Coercion into Apostasy: Effect on Marriage and Istihsan
Ruling on apostasy under coercion and its marital consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage continuity after coerced apostasy and explain the role of istihsan in accepting the husband's claim.

Core Idea
Coerced apostasy does not dissolve marriage because apostasy pertains to belief, and doubt cannot establish separation.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common juristic problem: what happens to a marriage when a person is forced to utter words of apostasy? The Hanafi school, using istihsan (juristic preference), rules that the marriage remains intact if the heart is firm in faith. The following discussion unpacks the proof and the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the core ruling: when one is coerced into apostasy, the wife does not become bain (irrevocably separated). This is the mufta bihi position.
The ruling applies specifically to ikrah (coercion) that overcomes the will.
2
Understand the dalil (proof)
Apostasy relates to belief (i'tiqad), not mere utterance. The text uses rational evidence: if the heart is tranquil with faith, the person is not a disbeliever. Doubt (shak) about inner belief prevents certainty in separation.
The proof is talil (rational) not a direct textual source.
3
Apply istihsan for the husband's statement
When the wife claims separation and the husband says his heart was faithful, his statement is accepted by istihsan. The words are not inherently for separation; separation requires a change of belief, which coercion does not indicate.
Istihsan deviates from strict analogy (qiyas) to favor the husband due to doubt.
4
Contrast with coercion into Islam
If a non-Muslim is coerced into Islam, he is judged Muslim in this world because Islam is elevated. The doubt is resolved in favor of Islam. However, if he later recants, he is not killed due to the same doubt.
This shows the principle: doubt repels capital punishment, but Islam is given precedence when both possibilities exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و اذا اكره على الردّة لم تبن امرأته منه لان الردة تتعلق بالاعتقاد الاترى انه لو كان قلبه مطمئنا بالايمان لا يكفر وفي اعتقاده الكفر شك فلا يثبت البينونة بالشك فان قالت المرأة قد بنتُ منك وقال هو قد اظهرت ذلك و قلبی مطمئن بالايمان فالقول قوله استحسانا لان اللفظ غير موضوع للفرقة وهى بتبدل الاعتقاد ومع الاكراه لا يدل على التبدل فكان القول قوله بخلاف الاكراه على الاسلام حيث يصير به مسلما لانه لما احتمل واحتمل رجّحنا الاسلام فى الحالين لانه يعلو ولا يُعلى وهذا بيان الحكم اما فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى اذا لم يعتقده فليس بمسلم ولو اكره على الاسلام حتى حكم باسلامه ثم رجع لم يقتل لتمكن الشبهة وهى دارئة للقتل
He said: And if he is coerced into apostasy, his wife does not become separated from him because apostasy relates to belief. Do you not see that if his heart is tranquil with faith, he does not disbelieve? And in his belief of unbelief there is doubt, so separation is not established by doubt. If the woman says, 'I have become separated from you,' and he says, 'I only uttered that while my heart was tranquil with faith,' then his statement is accepted by istihsan, because the word is not placed for separation; separation occurs by change of belief, and with coercion it does not indicate change. So his statement is accepted. Contrary to coercion into Islam, where he becomes Muslim by it, because when both probabilities exist, we give precedence to Islam in both situations, as it is high and not low. This is the statement of the ruling; as for between him and Allah Almighty, if he does not believe it, he is not a Muslim. And if he is coerced into Islam until his Islam is judged, then he recants, he is not killed due to the presence of doubt, and doubt repels killing.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the effect of coercion into apostasy on marriage. The text (likely from Al-Hidayah) states that if a person is forced to utter words of apostasy, his wife does not become irrevocably divorced (bain) because apostasy is fundamentally a matter of belief. The proof is rational (talil): if his heart remains firm in faith, he is not a disbeliever; and since his inner belief is uncertain due to coercion, the certainty of separation cannot be based on doubt. If the wife claims separation and the husband says he only spoke under duress with faith in his heart, his statement is accepted by istihsan (juristic preference). The reasoning is that the uttered words are not inherently grounds for separation; separation only occurs with a genuine change of belief, and coercion does not prove such a change. A contrast is made with coercion into Islam: if a non-Muslim is forced to utter the shahada, he is deemed a Muslim in worldly legal matters because Islam is elevated and given precedence when both possibilities exist. However, in the sight of Allah, if he did not believe, he is not a Muslim. If such a person later recants, he is not executed due to the doubt (shubha) arising from the coercion, as doubt averts capital punishment.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrāh): Coercion or compulsion that overcomes one's will, typically through threat of harm.
ردة (riddah): Apostasy from Islam, i.e., renouncing the faith by word or deed.
بينونة (baynūnah): Irrevocable separation (divorce) resulting in the wife becoming unlawful to the husband without a new marriage.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference, a method of reasoning that deviates from analogy in favor of convenience or fairness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person is coerced into uttering words of apostasy, what is the effect on his marriage?
A) The wife becomes bain (irrevocably divorced) immediately.
B) The wife does not become bain because apostasy relates to belief, and doubt prevents separation.
C) The marriage is suspended until the coerced person reaffirms faith.
D) The wife becomes bain only if she also converts.

Key Takeaway
Coerced apostasy does not dissolve marriage under Hanafi law because apostasy is a matter of belief, and doubt cannot establish separation.

Coercion and Intention: Legal vs. Conscience Rulings in Apostasy
Ruling on coerced person's later claims and effects on marital status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between hukman and diyanatan to statements made by a person coerced into uttering disbelief, and state the effect on marriage.

Core Idea
A coerced apostate's later claim about intention determines the marriage's dissolution legally, but not always before Allah.

In Hanafi fiqh, the distinction between legal ruling (hukman) and conscience (diyanatan) is crucial in cases of coercion. When a person is forced to utter words of disbelief, their subsequent explanation of their intention can lead to different outcomes for their marriage. This lesson unpacks two scenarios from the classical text, showing when the marriage dissolves only in the eyes of the court, and when it also dissolves as a matter of religious duty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the coerced act and the person's claim
The passage begins with a person coerced into uttering kufr. Note what he claims afterwards: either 'I was informing about a past event' or 'I intended what was demanded but also thought of informing'.
The claim determines the legal and conscience ruling.
2
Apply the rule: first claim (past event)
If he says he only informed about a past event, he is considered to have confessed to doing it willingly because he went beyond what was coerced. Hence, the marriage dissolves legally (hukman) but not in conscience (diyanatan).
The key is that he admitted to doing something voluntarily.
3
Apply the rule: second claim (intended demanded, thought of past)
If he says he intended what was demanded but also thought of informing, he is deemed to have initiated disbelief jestingly. Therefore, the marriage dissolves both legally and in conscience.
Jesting in matters of apostasy has full effect.
4
Extend to analogous cases
The rule is analogized to cases of coercion to prostrate to a cross or revile the Prophet. The same distinction applies: if the coerced person claims a secondary intention (e.g., intending prayer to Allah), divorce is only legal; if the thought crossed his mind, it is both legal and conscience.
The principle is generalizable to any coerced act of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال الذى اكره على اجراء كلمة الكفر اخبرت عن امر ماض ولم اكن فعلتُ بانت منه حكما لا ديانة لانه اقرانه طائع باتيان مالم يُكره عليه وحكم هذا الطائع ما ذكرناه ولو قال اردتُ ما طلب منى وقد خطر ببالي الخبر عما مضى بانت ديانة وقضاء لانه اقرانه مبتدىء بالكفر هازل به حيث علم لنفسه مخلصا غيره وعلى هذا اذا اكره على الصلاة للصليب وسبّ محمد النبي عليه السلام ففعل وقال نويت به الصلاة الله تعالى ومحمدًا آخر غير النبي عليه السلام بانت منه قضاء لا ديانة ولو صلى للصليب وسب محمداً النبي عليه السلام وقد خطر بباله الصلاة الله تعالى وسب غير النبي عليه السلام بانت منه ديانة وقضاء لمامر وقد قررناه زيادة على هذا في كفاية المنتهى والله اعلم
And if the one who was coerced into uttering the word of disbelief says, 'I was informing about a past event, and I did not do it,' then his wife becomes separated from him legally (hukman) but not in conscience (diyanatan), because he confessed that he did it willingly, doing what he was not coerced into, and the ruling for such a willing person is what we mentioned. And if he says, 'I intended what was demanded of me, but it crossed my mind to inform about the past,' then she becomes separated both in conscience and legally, because he confessed to initiating disbelief jestingly, since he knew for himself a way out other than that. Similarly, if he is coerced into prostrating to the cross and reviling the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), and he does so and says, 'I intended by it prayer to Allah Almighty and to a Muhammad other than the Prophet,' then she becomes separated from him legally but not in conscience. And if he prostrates to the cross and reviles the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and it crossed his mind to pray to Allah and revile someone other than the Prophet, then she becomes separated from him both in conscience and legally, as previously. And we have established more on this in Kifayat al-Muntaha. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal consequences when a person coerced into uttering disbelief later makes claims about his intention. The text presents two scenarios. In the first, the coerced person claims that he was only telling about a past event and did not actually do it. Here, the ruling is that his wife becomes bain (irrevocably divorced) in terms of the legal ruling (hukman) but not in terms of his inner conscience (diyanatan). The reason is that by saying 'I told about a past event,' he effectively admits that he willingly uttered the words of disbelief without genuine coercion (since coercion was for him to do it, not to say he did it). Thus, he is treated as a willing apostate. In the second scenario, he says he intended what was demanded but also had the thought of informing about the past. Here, she becomes bain both legally and in conscience because he confesses to initiating disbelief as a joke, knowing that this was a way out. The text then extends the same logic to cases of coercion into prostrating to a cross or reviling the Prophet. If the coerced person claims he intended worship of Allah and a different Muhammad, the marriage dissolves legally but not in conscience. If he claims the thought crossed his mind, it dissolves both ways. The text concludes by referencing Kifayat al-Muntaha for further detail.

Key Terms
حكماً (ḥukman): Legally or in terms of the external ruling, as opposed to inner reality.
ديانة (diyānatan): In terms of conscience or religious duty, considered before Allah.
طائع (ṭā’i‘): Willingly, voluntarily, as opposed to under coercion.
هازل (hāzil): Jesting or joking, implying intent without seriousness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The wife becomes bain both legally and in conscience.
B) The wife does not become bain at all.
C) The wife becomes bain legally but not in conscience.
D) The marriage is annulled retroactively.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a coerced apostate's later statement about intention determines whether the marriage dissolves only in the eyes of the law or also in conscience before Allah.

Three Scenarios of Coerced Utterance of Kufr
Explaining three scenarios of coerced kufr and effect on marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios of coerced utterance of kufr and determine for each whether the coerced person is a kafir judicially and/or religiously, and the effect on his marriage.

Core Idea
Coerced utterance of kufr does not make one an apostate if the heart is at peace with faith; the ruling depends on intention.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah's commentary on the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah) explains a critical Hanafi legal principle: the distinction between outward acts of kufr performed under duress and the inner state of faith. The text presents three distinct scenarios based on the coerced person's intention, and then extends the analysis to analogous acts like prostration to the cross or reviling the Prophet. Understanding these scenarios is essential for correctly applying the Hanafi position on coercion and apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the coercer's demand
Determine what the coerced person was forced to do: utter a word of kufr, prostrate to a cross, or revile the Prophet. The coerced person's response is then analyzed.
The act itself is external; the ruling hinges on internal intention.
2
Determine the coerced person's claimed intention
The coerced person may declare his intention in three ways: (1) his heart remained at peace with faith, no alternative thought; (2) he intended to give a false report of past kufr; (3) he intended to commit present kufr despite a chance to avoid it.
The key is whether he willingly aligned with the coercer's demand or not.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule to each scenario
Scenario 1: Not a kafir at all (neither qada'an nor diyanatan). Scenario 2: Kafir only qada'an (judicially) but not diyanatan (religiously). Scenario 3: Kafir both qada'an and diyanatan.
The Quranic verse 16:106 provides the basis: 'except him who is forced while his heart is at peace with faith.'
4
Determine the effect on marriage
In scenario 1, the wife does not become bain (irrevocably divorced). In scenario 2, she becomes bain only judicially (qada'an). In scenario 3, she becomes bain both judicially and religiously.
Judicial bainunah means the judge rules separation, but before Allah the marriage may still be valid in scenario 2.
5
Extend to analogous acts
The same three scenarios apply to coerced prostration to a cross or reviling the Prophet, with identical rulings based on the coerced person's intention.
The principle is general: coercion on any act of kufr is treated the same way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الاكراه

۲۵۰
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ۱۲ کیونکہ اس نے اپنے لئے اس کے علاوہ دوسرامخلص جان لیا۔ وعلی ہذا اگر صلیب کیلئے نماز پڑھنے یا نبی علیہ السلام کو بُرا کہنے پر مجبور کیا گیا اور اس نے ایسا کر لیا اور یہ کہا کہ میں نے اللہ کے لئے نماز کی اور نبی علیہ السلام کے علاوہ دوسرے محمد کی نیت کی تھی تو اس کی بیوی بائنہ ہو جائے گی ، قضاء نہ کہ دیائے ۔ اور اگر صلیب کیلئے نماز پڑھی اور نبی علیہ السلام کو برا کہا حالانکہ اس کے دل میں اللہ کیلئے نماز کا اور غیر نبی علیہ السلام کی بدگوئی کا خیال آیا تو اس کی بیوی بائنہ ہو جائے گی دیانیہ بھی اور قضاء بھی بدلیل مذکور ۔ اور ہم نے اس سے زیادہ تو ضیح کی ہے اس کی کفایتہ المنتہی میں واللہ اعلم ۔
