Book of Coercion. 250. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah Volume 12. Because he considered for himself besides that another escape. And upon this, if he is coerced to pray for the cross or to speak ill of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he did so, and he said: 'I prayed for Allah and intended another Muhammad besides the Prophet,' then his wife becomes bain (irrevocably divorced) judicially, not religiously. And if he prayed for the cross and spoke ill of the Prophet while the thought of praying for Allah and reviling someone other than the Prophet came to his heart, then his wife becomes bain both religiously and judicially due to the mentioned evidence. And we have explained more than this in Kifayat al-Muntahi, and Allah knows best. Explanation of his statement: 'And if he said: The one who was coerced...' It is attached to 'he said: I have displayed that.' The meaning is that the person who was coerced to utter a word of kufr said in response to the wife: 'I intended by the word of kufr that I uttered at the command of the coercer to give a false report of a past time, i.e., that I committed kufr at some time, although this report is false because I never committed kufr.' In this case, his wife becomes bain judicially, not religiously. And the reason for becoming bain judicially is that he admitted that he willingly uttered a word for which he was not coerced, because the coercion was for manifesting kufr, and reporting is contrary to manifesting. His statement: 'And if he said: I intended...' And if the aforementioned person said: 'I intended what the coercer demanded, although the news of a past event came to my heart,' then in this case his wife becomes bain both judicially and religiously. Because when the thought of giving a false report of a past matter came to his heart, if he had intended that, he would have been saved from kufr; despite that, he intended what the coercer intended, so he admitted that he initiated kufr willingly. Thus it is like voluntarily uttering the word of kufr, so his wife becomes bain in every respect. Benefit: The outcome of the mentioned issue is that the person who is coerced to utter a word of kufr has three scenarios. In one scenario, he does not become a kafir neither judicially nor religiously: that is when he uttered the word of kufr (but without any intention other than complying with coercion?) and no thought except that on which he was coerced came to his heart, rather his heart remained at peace with faith. In the second scenario, he becomes a kafir only judicially, not religiously: that is when he intended to give a false report of a past matter, not intending future kufr. In the third scenario, he becomes a kafir both judicially and religiously: that is when the thought of giving a false report of a past matter comes to his heart, but he does not choose it; rather he intends future kufr according to the coercer's command. His statement: 'And upon this, if he is coerced...' If someone is coerced to prostrate to the cross or to revile the honorable status of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he does so, then like the previous issue, there are also three scenarios: (1) The coerced person says: 'I intended the prostration in prayer for Allah,' or 'in the reviling I intended a Christian named Muhammad.' In this case, he becomes a kafir judicially, because when he said 'I intended the prayer for Allah,' he admitted that what occurred from him was not under coercion. But religiously he is not a kafir, because he prostrated for Allah, not for the cross, and he did not revile the Prophet. (2) The coerced person says: 'The thought of praying for Allah or of the Christian named Muhammad came to my heart, but I prostrated to the cross or reviled the Prophet as per the coercer's command, though I was not pleased with it.' In this case also he becomes a kafir, because he found an opportunity to escape kufr, yet he acted according to the coercer's intention, and his saying 'I was not pleased' is useless. So this is kufr both judicially and religiously. (3) The coerced person says: 'No thought came to my heart, and I prostrated to the cross or reviled the Prophet under compulsion.' In this case, he is not a kafir, neither judicially nor religiously, because he did it under duress, his heart being at peace. besturdubooks.wordpress.com It is like the case of one who prays in front of a cross without coercion from anyone and says: 'I intended the prayer for Allah' – he is believed religiously, not judicially. Likewise this. 13-
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah's commentary on the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah) addresses a critical Hanafi legal principle: coerced utterance of words of kufr does not automatically render a person an apostate; the ruling depends on the state of the heart. The commentator expands on the Hidayah text, presenting three distinct scenarios for a person coerced into uttering kufr. In the first scenario, the coerced person merely complies with the external act while his heart remains firmly attached to faith (qalbuhu mutma'inna bil iman). In this case, he is not considered a kafir at all – neither in the judicial sense (qada'an) nor in the religious sense (diyanatan). This is based on the Quranic verse (16:106) which exempts those who are coerced while their heart is at peace with faith. In the second scenario, the person claims that he intended to give a false report about a past act of kufr (ikhbar 'an madin kadhiban) rather than actually committing kufr in the present. The Hanafi ruling is that judicially (qada'an) his wife becomes bain (irrevocably divorced), because he admitted that he willingly uttered a word that was not under coercion – the coercion was to manifest kufr, but a report about the past is not manifesting kufr in the present. However, religiously (diyanatan) he remains faithful because in his heart he did not intend present kufr. In the third scenario, the person admits that the thought of giving a false report occurred to him, but he did not adopt it; instead, he intended to obey the coercer's demand for present kufr. Here he becomes a kafir both judicially and religiously, and his wife is bain in every respect. The commentator then extends the same analysis to analogous acts: prostration to a cross (sujud lial-salib) and reviling the Prophet (sabb al-nabi). If the coerced person claims he intended to pray to Allah or intended a different Muhammad (a Christian), then judicially he is a kafir (because he admitted he was not under coercion in that specific act), but religiously he is not (since his intention was pure). If he says he had a pure thought but acted according to the coercer's will, then both judicially and religiously he is a kafir. If he says no thought crossed his mind and he acted under pure duress, then he is not a kafir at all. The passage emphasizes that the Hanafi school gives great weight to internal intention (niyyah) and distinguishes between the external act and internal belief. The phrase 'qada'an' refers to the ruling of a judge in a court, which is based on external manifestations; 'diyanatan' refers to the person's own conscience and accountability before Allah. Thus a person might be considered a Muslim before Allah while being treated as an apostate in court due to his external admission.

Key Terms
اکراہ (ikrāh): Coercion that overcomes a person's will, preventing voluntary action; in this context, it refers to threats that compel utterance of kufr.
قضاء (qaḍā'an): Judicially; the ruling of a judge based on external evidence and admissions, regardless of inner reality.
دیانة (diyānatan): Religiously; according to one's conscience and accountability before Allah, based on intention.
بائنہ (bā'inah): Irrevocable divorce; the wife becomes forbidden to the husband without a new marriage contract.
مطمئن بالایمان (muṭma'inna bil īmān): Heart at peace and content with faith, not affected by the external coercion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second scenario of coerced utterance of kufr (intending a false past report), what is the ruling on the coerced person's iman and marriage?
A) He is a kafir both judicially and religiously; wife becomes bain in both senses.
B) He is a kafir only judicially; wife becomes bain only judicially.
C) He is not a kafir at all; marriage remains valid.
D) He is a kafir only religiously; wife becomes bain only religiously.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on coerced utterance of kufr depends on the person's intention: if the heart is at peace with faith, no apostasy; if a false past report is intended, only judicial apostasy; if present kufr is intended despite a chance to avoid it, full apostasy.

Marriage of a Safih and Limitation on Mahr
Ruling on marriage and mahr for a safih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the validity of a safih's marriage, apply the rule that mahr is limited to mahr al-mithl, and derive the consequence for talaq before consummation.

Core Idea
A safih's marriage is valid; mahr is limited to mahr al-mithl; any excess is void.

A safih is a person under interdiction due to prodigality, but marriage is considered an essential need. The Hanafi school validates his marriage and restricts the mahr to the customary amount to prevent waste. This lesson covers the rulings on marriage, mahr, and the effect of divorce before consummation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the legal status of a safih's marriage. The passage states it is valid (jāz).
Jest does not affect marriage, and it is an essential need.
2
Apply the mahr limitation
When a safih names a mahr, only the amount of mahr al-mithl is valid. The excess is void because there is no necessity and the safih has no valid consideration.
Analogous to a person on his deathbed (mard al-mawt) who cannot bequeath more than one-third.
3
Analyze the case of talaq before consummation
If the safih divorces before consummation, half of the named mahr (up to mahr al-mithl) is due from his wealth, because the naming is valid to that extent.
The half is calculated based on the valid portion, not the entire named mahr.
4
Confirm the permissibility of multiple marriages
The safih may marry up to four women, or one every day, based on the same principle of necessity.
No additional restrictions beyond normal polygyny limits.

Source Text

سفیہ مجور کا عورت سے نکاح کرنے کا حکم
قال وان تزوج امرأة جاز نكاحها لانه لا يؤثر فيه الهزل ولانه من حوائجه الاصلية وان سمى لها مهرا جاز منه مقدار مهر مثلها لانه من ضرورات النكاح وبطل الفضل لانه لا ضرورة فيه وهو التزام بالتسمية ولا نظر له فيه فلم تصح الزيادة فصار كالمريض مرض الموت ولو طلقها قبل الدخول بها وجب لها النصف في ماله لان التسمية صحيحة الى مقدار مهر المثل وكذا اذا تزوج باربع نسوة او كل يوم واحدة لما بينا قال. ويخرج الزكواة من مال السفيه لانها واجبة عليه وينفق على اولاده وزوجته ومن تجب نفقته عليه من ذوى ارحامه لان احياء ولده وزوجته من حوائجه والانفاق على ذى الرحم واجب عليه حقا لقرابته والسفه لا يبطل حقوق الناس الا ان القاضى يدفع قدر الزكوة اليه ليصرفها الى مصرفها لانه لابد من نيته لكونها عبادة لكن بعث امينا معه كيلا يصرفه فى غير وجهه وفى النفقة يدفع الى امينه ليصرفها لانها ليست بعبادة فلا يحتاج الى نبته وهذا بخلاف ما اذا حلف او نذر او ظاهر حيث لا يلزمه المال بل يكفر يمينه وظهاره بالصوم لانه مما يجب بفعله فلو فحنا هذا الباب يُبدِّرُ امواله بهذا الطريق ولا كذلك ما يجب ابتداء بغير فعله قال فان اراد حجة الاسلام لم يمنع منها لانها واجبة عليه بايجاب الله تعالى من غير صنعه ولا يسلم
Heading: Ruling on a safih under interdiction marrying a woman. He said: And if he marries a woman, his marriage is valid because jest does not affect it, and because it is one of his essential needs. And if he names a mahr for her, the amount of her mahr al-mithl is valid from him because it is among the necessities of marriage, and the excess is void because there is no necessity in it, and it is a commitment by naming, and he has no consideration in it, so the increase is not valid. Thus he becomes like a person sick with mortal illness. And if he divorces her before consummation, half of it becomes obligatory in his wealth, because the naming is valid up to the amount of mahr al-mithl. Similarly, if he marries four women or one every day, due to what we have explained. He said: And zakah is taken out from the wealth of the safih because it is obligatory upon him. And he spends on his children, his wife, and those of his relatives whose maintenance is obligatory upon him, because keeping his children and wife alive is among his needs, and spending on relatives is obligatory upon him as a right due to kinship. And safah does not nullify the rights of people. However, the judge gives the amount of zakah to him so that he may disburse it to its recipients, because his intention is necessary as it is an act of worship. But he sends a trustworthy person with him so that he does not spend it in other than its due. And in the case of nafaqah, it is given to his trustee to spend, because it is not an act of worship and does not require his intention. And this is contrary to if he swears an oath or makes a vow or does zihar, where wealth is not incumbent upon him; rather, he expiates his oath and zihar by fasting, because that is something that becomes obligatory by his action. If we were to open this door, he would waste his wealth in this way. And not so with that which becomes obligatory initially without his action. He said: And if he intends Hajj al-Islam, he is not prevented from it, because it is obligatory upon him by the command of Allah Most High, not by his own doing. And he does not give [the wealth] to him? [The text ends abruptly.]
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on a safih (under interdiction) regarding marriage and financial obligations. Marriage is valid because it is an essential need and jest does not affect it. The mahr is limited to mahr al-mithl (the customary dowry for a woman of her status); any excess is void because the safih cannot commit to unnecessary expenditure. If he divorces before consummation, half of the mahr (up to mahr al-mithl) is due from his wealth. He may marry up to four women. Zakah is taken out from his wealth by the judge, but the safih himself must make the intention, so the judge gives the zakah money to him with a trustee to ensure proper disbursement. For nafaqah (maintenance) of his dependents, it is given directly to his trustee because intention is not required. This distinguishes obligations that arise from his own actions (like oaths, vows, zihar) where he must expiate by fasting rather than paying, to prevent him from wasting wealth. Hajj al-Islam is not prevented because it is a divine obligation not initiated by his own action. The text ends with 'wa la yuslam' (and he does not surrender?), which might be cut off.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The customary dowry for a woman of similar status to the bride.
سفیہ (safih): A person under judicial interdiction due to prodigality or incompetence in handling wealth.
مریض مرض الموت (marīḍ maraḍ al-mawt): A person sick with a mortal illness who is under restriction in gratuitous dispositions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the excess mahr over mahr al-mithl void when a safih names a mahr?
A) Because the safih is compelled to marry.
B) Because there is no necessity in the excess, and the safih has no valid consideration in it, similar to a person on his deathbed.
C) Because the judge does not allow any mahr.
D) Because the marriage itself is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A safih's marriage is valid, but his mahr is capped at the customary value to protect his wealth.

Zakah, Nafaqah, and Obligations of a Safih
Rulings on zakah, nafaqah, and hajj for a safih, and distinction with self-initiated obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how zakah and nafaqah are handled for a safih, and differentiate between obligations arising from his own action (e.g., oaths) and those imposed by divine command (e.g., zakah, hajj).

Core Idea
Zakah and nafaqah are taken from a safih's wealth with procedural safeguards; obligations from his own actions are expiated by fasting, not payment.

A safih under interdiction still has obligations like zakah and nafaqah. However, the judge oversees the disbursement to prevent waste. This lesson also clarifies why certain obligations (like expiation for oaths) are fulfilled by fasting instead of paying, while divinely imposed obligations like zakah and hajj are not prevented.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligations that remain binding on a safih
Zakah, nafaqah (for dependents), and hajj are obligatory despite the safih's interdiction.
These are obligations 'not from his own action' but divinely imposed.
2
Understand the procedure for zakah
The judge gives the zakah amount to the safih to make the intention (niyyah) because zakah is an act of worship, but a trustee accompanies him to ensure proper disbursement.
Niyyah is required for worship; trustee prevents misuse.
3
Understand the procedure for nafaqah
Nafaqah is given directly to the safih's trustee because intention is not required; it is merely a financial right.
Contrast with zakah: worship vs financial duty.
4
Differentiate obligations arising from the safih's own actions
If the safih swears an oath, makes a vow, or performs zihar, he does not pay money; rather, he expiates by fasting. This prevents him from wasting wealth through his own actions.
Obligations from his own actions are restricted to non-financial expiation.
5
Apply the rule to hajj
Hajj al-Islam is not prevented because it is an obligation from Allah, not from the safih's own doing. He is allowed to go, and the judge ensures proper spending.
Hajj is not initiated by his action but by the divine command.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

The Marriage of a Safih: Valid Without Guardian, Mahr Capped at Mahru'l-Mithl
Ruling on safih's marriage validity and mahr limit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a safih's marriage, explain the limit on mahr, and evaluate the opposing school's argument.

Core Idea
A safih's marriage is valid without guardian because jest doesn't affect marriage, but his mahr is capped at mahru'l-mithl.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a key intersection of marital and guardianship law. A safih (foolish person under interdiction) is normally restricted from financial transactions, but the Hanafi school treats marriage differently – as a primary need that cannot be blocked by interdiction. The mahr, however, is limited to prevent wasteful excess, analogizing the safih to a terminally ill patient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The safih's marriage is valid. Why is it not voided by his foolishness? The passage states two reasons: jest does not affect marriage, and marriage is a primary need.
2
Locate the evidence
The Hanafi school relies on two proofs: jest does not affect marriage (implying that even if the safih joked, it's valid), and marriage is a primary need of the safih.
The hadith: 'Three things are serious in earnest and in jest: marriage, divorce, and manumission' is the basis for the first proof.
3
Note the inter-madhhab dispute
Malik, Shafi'i, and a Hanbali scholar argue that marriage is a compensatory contract like purchase, requiring guardian permission.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the seriousness of marriage overrides any foolish intent, and the need for marriage is fundamental, so interdiction cannot prevent it.
The analogy to purchase is rejected because marriage is not purely financial.
5
Apply the mahr limit
The safih's specified mahr is valid only up to mahru'l-mithl; excess is void. This mirrors the rule for a terminally ill patient who cannot give away more than one-third.
The same limit applies even if he marries multiple women in succession.

قال وان تزوج امرأة جاز نكاحها لانه لا يؤثر فيه الهزل ولانه من حوائجه الاصلية وان سمى لها مهرا جاز منه مقدار مهر مثلها لانه من ضرورات النكاح وبطل الفضل لانه لا ضرورة فيه وهو التزام بالتسمية ولا نظر له فيه فلم تصح الزيادة فصار كالمريض مرض الموت ولو طلقها قبل الدخول بها وجب لها النصف في ماله لان التسمية صحيحة الى مقدار مهر المثل وكذا اذا تزوج باربع نسوة او كل يوم واحدة لما بينا قال. ويخرج الزكواة من مال السفيه لانها واجبة عليه وينفق على اولاده وزوجته ومن تجب نفقته عليه من ذوى ارحامه لان احياء ولده وزوجته من حوائجه والانفاق على ذى الرحم واجب عليه حقا لقرابته والسفه لا يبطل حقوق الناس الا ان القاضى يدفع قدر الزكوة اليه ليصرفها الى مصرفها لانه لابد من نيته لكونها عبادة لكن بعث امينا معه كيلا يصرفه فى غير وجهه وفى النفقة يدفع الى امينه ليصرفها لانها ليست بعبادة فلا يحتاج الى نبته وهذا بخلاف ما اذا حلف او نذر او ظاهر حيث لا يلزمه المال بل يكفر يمينه وظهاره بالصوم لانه مما يجب بفعمله فلو فحنا هذا الباب يُبدِّرُ امواله بهذا الطريق ولا كذلك ما يجب ابتداء بغير فعله قال فان اراد حجة الاسلام لم يمنع منها لانها واجبة عليه بايجاب الله تعالى من غير صنعه ولا يسلم القاضى النفقة اليها ويسلمها الى ثقة من الحاج يُنفقها عليه فى طريق الحج كيلا يتلفها في غير هذا الوجه
ترجمہ اگر سفیہ مجور نے کسی عورت سے نکاح کر لیا تو اس کا نکاح جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ نکاح میں ہنزل موثر نہیں ہوتا اور اسلئے کہ نکاح اس کی اصلی ضرورتوں میں سے ہے اور اگر اس کیلئے کچھ مہر مقرر کیا تو اس کے مہر مثل کے بقدر جائز ہوگا۔ کیونکہ یہ ضروریات نکاح میں سے ہے اور زائد مقدار باطل ہوگی۔ کیونکہ اس کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں اور اس نے بیان کر کے اپنے ذمہ لازم کر لیا ہے۔ حالانکہ اس میں اس کی بہتری نہیں ہے تو زیادتی صحیح نہ ہوگی تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے مرض الموت کا مریض اور اگر اس کو طلاق دے دی دخول سے پہلے تو عورت کیلئے سفیہ کے مال سے نصف مہر مثل واجب ہوگا کیونکہ مہر مثل کی مقدار تک تسمیہ صحیح ہے۔ ایسے ہی اگر چار عورتوں سے نکاح کیا یا ہر روز ایک عورت سے نکاح کیا۔ بدلیل مذکور اور نکالی جائے گی زکوۃ سفیہ کے مال سے۔ کیونکہ وہ اس پر واجب ہے اور خرچ کیا جائے گا اس کی اولاد، اس کی بیوی اور ان لوگوں پر جن کا نفقہ اس پر واجب ہے اس کے ذوی الارحام میں سے، کیونکہ اس کی اولاد اور بیوی کا زندہ رکھنا اس کی ضروریات میں سے ہے اور زورحم پر خرچ کرنا حق قرابت کی وجہ سے اس پر واجب ہے اور سفاہت لوگوں کے حقوق کو باطل نہیں کرتی ۔ لیکن قاضی بقدر زکوۃ لے کر اس کو دیدے تا کہ وہ اس کو مصارف زکوۃ میں خرچ کرلے۔ کیونکہ اس کی نیت کا ہونا ضروری ہے، زکوۃ کے عبادت ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ لیکن قاضی اس کے پاس اپنا امین بھیج دے تا کہ وہ بے طریقہ خرچ نہ کر ڈالے۔ اور نفقہ کی صورت میں اپنے امین کو دے دے تا کہ وہ صرف کر لے۔ کیونکہ نفقہ عبادت نہیں ہے تو اس کی نیت کی ضرورت نہیں۔ اور یہ اس کے خلاف ہے۔ جب سفیہ نے قسم کھائی یا نذر کی یا ظہار کیا کہ اس پر مال لازم نہ ہوگا بلکہ قسم وظہار کا کفارہ روزہ سے ادا کریگا۔ کیونکہ یہ ان چیزوں میں سے ہے جو اسکے فعل سے واجب ہوئی ہے۔ پس اگر ہم یہ در اوزہ کھول دیں تو وہ اسی طریقہ سے اپنا مال فضول خرچ کرے گا۔ اور جو اس کے فعل کے بغیر ابتداء واجب ہو وہ ایسا نہیں ہے۔ اگر سفیہ نے حج اسلام کا ارادہ کیا تو اس سے منع نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ حج اس پر اس کے فعل کے بغیر بایجاب خداوندی واجب ہے لیکن قاضی حج کا خرچ اس کو نہ دے بلکہ کسی معتمد حاجی کے سپرد کر دے جو حج کے راستہ میں اس پر خرچ کرتا جائے تا کہ سفیہ اس نفقہ کو بے راہ تلف نہ کر ڈالے۔
تشريح ... قوله وان تزوج امرأة .... الخ - اگر سفیہ مجور نے کسی عورت سے نکاح کر لیا تو ہمارے اور امام احمد کے نزدیک اس کا نکاح جائز ہے۔ امام مالک، امام شافعی اور ابو الخطاب خیلی کے نزدیک اجازت ولی کے بغیر جائز نہیں کیونکہ شراء کی طرح نکاح بھی عقد معاوضہ ہے تو اجازت ولی کے بغیر جائز نہ ہوگا۔ ہم یہ کہتے ہیں کہ نکاح میں ہنرل موثر نہیں ہوتا تو حجر بھی موثر نہ ہوگا۔ نیز اسلئے بھی کہ نکاح اس کی اصلی ضروریات میں سے ہے۔
قوله وكذا اذا تزوج باربع ... الخ اسی طرح اگر سفیہ مجور نے بعقد واحد چار عورتوں سے نکاح کیا۔ یا ہر روز ایک عورت سے نکاح کرتا رہا بایں طور کہ آج ایک عورت سے مہر پر نکاح کیا اور اس کو طلاق دے کر دوسرے روز دوسری عورت سے نکاح کر لیا۔ اسی طرح کئی بار کیا۔ تب بھی نکاح جائز ہے اور مہر کا بیان بقدر مہر مثل صحیح ہوگا اور زائد مقدار باطل ہوگی ۔ وجہ وہی ہے کہ نکاح اس کی ضروریات میں
سے ہے۔
فائده ... امام ابو حنیفہ نے اسی مسئلہ سے احتجاج کیا ہے کہ سفیہ پر حجر کرنا بے سود ہے۔ کیونکہ اس سے اتلاف مال کا انسداد نہیں ہوتا ۔
He said: If he marries a woman, his marriage is valid, because jest does not affect it, and because it is from his primary needs. If he specifies a mahr for her, from him is valid the amount of her mahru'l-mithl, because it is from the necessities of marriage, and the excess is void because there is no necessity in it, and it is an undertaking by specification and he has no discretion in it, so the increase is not valid; thus he becomes like a person terminally ill. If he divorces her before consummation, half of the mahru'l-mithl is obligatory for her in his wealth, because the specification is valid up to the amount of mahru'l-mithl. Similarly, if he marries four women or one every day, due to what we discussed. He said: Zakat is taken out from the safih's wealth because it is obligatory upon him. He spends on his children, his wife, and those whose nafaqah is obligatory upon him from his relatives, because preserving his children and wife is from his needs, and spending on the relative is obligatory upon him as a right of kinship. Foolishness does not nullify people's rights. However, the judge gives the amount of zakat to him to spend it in its due channel, because his intention is necessary due to it being worship, but he sends a trustworthy person with him so he does not spend it in other ways. For nafaqah, he gives it to his trustee to spend it, because it is not worship so intention is not needed. This is contrary to if he swears an oath, makes a nadhr, or engages in zihar: then property is not binding on him; rather he expiates his oath and zihar by fasting, because it is something obligatory by his action. If we open this door, he will waste his wealth in this way, not so for that which is obligatory initially without his action. He said: If he intends Hajj al-Islam, he is not prevented from it, because it is obligatory upon him by Allah's command without his action. The judge does not hand over the nafaqah to her [for Hajj?], but gives it to a trustworthy Hajji to spend on him during the Hajj journey so he does not waste it in other ways. Translation: If an interdicted safih marries a woman, his marriage is valid because jest does not affect it, and because marriage is from his primary needs. If he specifies a mahr, the amount of mahru'l-mithl is valid, and the excess is void because there is no necessity in it, and he has undertaken it by specification while there is no benefit in it, so the increase is not valid, thus he becomes like a terminally ill person. If he divorces her before consummation, half of mahru'l-mithl is obligatory for the woman from the safih's wealth because the specification is valid up to mahru'l-mithl. Similarly, if he marries four women or one every day, due to the mentioned evidence. Zakat is taken out from the safih's wealth because it is obligatory upon him. He spends on his children, wife, and those whose nafaqah is obligatory upon him from his relatives, because preserving his children and wife is from his needs, and spending on relatives is obligatory as a right of kinship. Foolishness does not nullify people's rights. But the judge gives the amount of zakat to him to spend in its due channel because his intention is necessary due to it being worship, but he sends a trustee with him so he does not spend it improperly. For nafaqah, he gives it to his trustee to spend because it is not worship, so intention is not needed. This is contrary to if he swears an oath, makes a nadhr, or does zihar: then property is not obligatory on him; rather he expiates his oath and zihar by fasting, because it is obligatory by his action. If we open this door, he will waste his wealth in this way; not so for what is obligatory initially without his action. If the safih intends Hajj al-Islam, he is not prevented because it is obligatory upon him by Allah's command without his action. The judge does not give the nafaqah to him but entrusts it to a trustworthy Hajji to spend on him during the journey so that the safih does not waste it. Commentary ... His statement: 'If he marries a woman...' According to us and Imam Ahmad, his marriage is valid. According to Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, and Abu al-Khattab al-Khalili, it is not valid without the permission of a guardian, because marriage is a compensatory contract like purchase, so it is not valid without guardian's permission. We say: Jest does not affect marriage, so interdiction also does not affect it. Also because marriage is from his primary needs. His statement: 'Similarly if he marries four...' Similarly, if the interdicted safih marries four women in one contract, or marries one woman each day, such that today he marries one woman with a mahr, divorces her, and the next day marries another, and so on many times, still the marriage is valid and the mahr specification up to mahru'l-mithl is valid, and the excess is void. The reason is that marriage is from his needs. Benefit: Imam Abu Hanifa argued from this issue that interdiction on a safih is useless, because it does not prevent wastage of wealth.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling on the marriage of a safih (a foolish person under interdiction). It begins with the ruling statement that such a marriage is valid because marriage is not affected by jest and because it is a primary need. The mahr specified is valid only up to the amount of mahru'l-mithl (the customary mahr for a woman of her status); any excess is void because the safih has no discretion in superfluous matters. This is analogized to a terminally ill patient who cannot give away property beyond one-third. If he divorces before consummation, half of mahru'l-mithl is obligatory. This applies even if he marries four women in one contract or one each day. The text then discusses zakat and nafaqah: zakat must be paid because it is a right of Allah; the judge gives the zakat amount to the safih for him to pay with intention, but with a trustee to prevent misuse. Nafaqah for dependents is given to a trustee directly because it is not worship requiring intention. The text contrasts voluntary obligations like oaths, nadhr, and zihar, where the safih is not required to pay money but can expiate with fasting, because these arise from his own actions and allowing money payment would lead to waste. Finally, Hajj is obligatory but the judge entrusts the expenses to a reliable person. The commentary (tashrih) includes an inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafis and Ahmad bin Hanbal consider the marriage valid without a guardian, while Malik, Shafi'i, and Abu al-Khattab require a guardian's permission, arguing that marriage is a compensatory contract like purchase. The Hanafi response is that jest does not affect marriage (so interdiction based on foolishness also doesn't), and marriage is a primary need. The passage ends with a benefit (fa'ida) quoting Imam Abu Hanifa that interdiction is futile because it doesn't prevent wealth wastage. This lesson covers the complete legal unit of the safih's marriage and its related consequences.

Key Terms
هزل (hazl): Jest or frivolity; a statement made in jest that lacks serious intent.
مهر مثل (mahru'l-mithl): The customary mahr for a woman of the same status as the bride.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction; a legal restriction on a person's ability to conduct transactions due to foolishness or other reasons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a safih marries a woman and specifies a mahr above the mahru'l-mithl, what happens to the excess?
A) The entire mahr is valid
B) The excess is void and must be returned
C) The marriage is invalid
D) The safih must pay the full amount from his guardian's wealth

Key Takeaway
A safih's marriage is valid without guardian, and his mahr is limited to mahru'l-mithl; any excess is void.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
7 min
Financial Obligations of a Safih: Zakat, Nafaqah, and Hajj
Rulings on financial obligations and expiations for a safih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school handles zakat, nafaqah, and hajj for a safih, and distinguish between acts that require monetary payment and those that do not.

Core Idea
A safih must pay zakat and nafaqah through a trustee, but expiates oaths by fasting; his interdiction does not nullify obligatory rights.

After establishing the validity of a safih's marriage and the mahr cap, the same passage in Al-Hidayah turns to his other financial obligations. Zakat and nafaqah are rights of Allah and people that cannot be avoided by interdiction, but the judge oversees the payments to prevent waste. In contrast, voluntary acts like oaths and nadhr that originate from the safih's own action are switched to a non-monetary form (fasting). Hajj, as a purely divine obligation not caused by him, is also allowed but managed through a trustee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligations discussed
The passage discusses zakat, nafaqah (for family and relatives), and hajj as obligatory duties. It also contrasts oaths, nadhr, and zihar.
2
Determine the method of payment for zakat
The judge gives the zakat amount to the safih with a trustee so the safih can make the necessary intention (niyyah).
Zakat is an act of worship requiring intention.
3
Determine the method for nafaqah
The judge gives nafaqah directly to a trustee to distribute, because nafaqah is not an act of worship and does not require intention from the safih.
4
Contrast with oaths, nadhr, and zihar
These are caused by the safih's own action; to prevent waste, he must expiate by fasting (no money), because allowing monetary payment would lead to depletion of his wealth.
5
Apply to hajj
Hajj is obligatory without his action; the judge does not give the money to him but to a trustworthy pilgrim to spend on him during the journey.

قال وان تزوج امرأة جاز نكاحها لانه لا يؤثر فيه الهزل ولانه من حوائجه الاصلية وان سمى لها مهرا جاز منه مقدار مهر مثلها لانه من ضرورات النكاح وبطل الفضل لانه لا ضرورة فيه وهو التزام بالتسمية ولا نظر له فيه فلم تصح الزيادة فصار كالمريض مرض الموت ولو طلقها قبل الدخول بها وجب لها النصف في ماله لان التسمية صحيحة الى مقدار مهر المثل وكذا اذا تزوج باربع نسوة او كل يوم واحدة لما بينا قال. ويخرج الزكواة من مال السفيه لانها واجبة عليه وينفق على اولاده وزوجته ومن تجب نفقته عليه من ذوى ارحامه لان احياء ولده وزوجته من حوائجه والانفاق على ذى الرحم واجب عليه حقا لقرابته والسفه لا يبطل حقوق الناس الا ان القاضى يدفع قدر الزكوة اليه ليصرفها الى مصرفها لانه لابد من نيته لكونها عبادة لكن بعث امينا معه كيلا يصرفه فى غير وجهه وفى النفقة يدفع الى امينه ليصرفها لانها ليست بعبادة فلا يحتاج الى نبته وهذا بخلاف ما اذا حلف او نذر او ظاهر حيث لا يلزمه المال بل يكفر يمينه وظهاره بالصوم لانه مما يجب بفعمله فلو فحنا هذا الباب يُبدِّرُ امواله بهذا الطريق ولا كذلك ما يجب ابتداء بغير فعله قال فان اراد حجة الاسلام لم يمنع منها لانها واجبة عليه بايجاب الله تعالى من غير صنعه ولا يسلم القاضى النفقة اليها ويسلمها الى ثقة من الحاج يُنفقها عليه فى طريق الحج كيلا يتلفها في غير هذا الوجه
ترجمہ اگر سفیہ مجور نے کسی عورت سے نکاح کر لیا تو اس کا نکاح جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ نکاح میں ہنزل موثر نہیں ہوتا اور اسلئے کہ نکاح اس کی اصلی ضرورتوں میں سے ہے اور اگر اس کیلئے کچھ مہر مقرر کیا تو اس کے مہر مثل کے بقدر جائز ہوگا۔ کیونکہ یہ ضروریات نکاح میں سے ہے اور زائد مقدار باطل ہوگی۔ کیونکہ اس کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں اور اس نے بیان کر کے اپنے ذمہ لازم کر لیا ہے۔ حالانکہ اس میں اس کی بہتری نہیں ہے تو زیادتی صحیح نہ ہوگی تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے مرض الموت کا مریض اور اگر اس کو طلاق دے دی دخول سے پہلے تو عورت کیلئے سفیہ کے مال سے نصف مہر مثل واجب ہوگا کیونکہ مہر مثل کی مقدار تک تسمیہ صحیح ہے۔ ایسے ہی اگر چار عورتوں سے نکاح کیا یا ہر روز ایک عورت سے نکاح کیا۔ بدلیل مذکور اور نکالی جائے گی زکوۃ سفیہ کے مال سے۔ کیونکہ وہ اس پر واجب ہے اور خرچ کیا جائے گا اس کی اولاد، اس کی بیوی اور ان لوگوں پر جن کا نفقہ اس پر واجب ہے اس کے ذوی الارحام میں سے، کیونکہ اس کی اولاد اور بیوی کا زندہ رکھنا اس کی ضروریات میں سے ہے اور زورحم پر خرچ کرنا حق قرابت کی وجہ سے اس پر واجب ہے اور سفاہت لوگوں کے حقوق کو باطل نہیں کرتی ۔ لیکن قاضی بقدر زکوۃ لے کر اس کو دیدے تا کہ وہ اس کو مصارف زکوۃ میں خرچ کرلے۔ کیونکہ اس کی نیت کا ہونا ضروری ہے، زکوۃ کے عبادت ہونے کی وجہ سے۔ لیکن قاضی اس کے پاس اپنا امین بھیج دے تا کہ وہ بے طریقہ خرچ نہ کر ڈالے۔ اور نفقہ کی صورت میں اپنے امین کو دے دے تا کہ وہ صرف کر لے۔ کیونکہ نفقہ عبادت نہیں ہے تو اس کی نیت کی ضرورت نہیں۔ اور یہ اس کے خلاف ہے۔ جب سفیہ نے قسم کھائی یا نذر کی یا ظہار کیا کہ اس پر مال لازم نہ ہوگا بلکہ قسم وظہار کا کفارہ روزہ سے ادا کریگا۔ کیونکہ یہ ان چیزوں میں سے ہے جو اسکے فعل سے واجب ہوئی ہے۔ پس اگر ہم یہ در اوزہ کھول دیں تو وہ اسی طریقہ سے اپنا مال فضول خرچ کرے گا۔ اور جو اس کے فعل کے بغیر ابتداء واجب ہو وہ ایسا نہیں ہے۔ اگر سفیہ نے حج اسلام کا ارادہ کیا تو اس سے منع نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ حج اس پر اس کے فعل کے بغیر بایجاب خداوندی واجب ہے لیکن قاضی حج کا خرچ اس کو نہ دے بلکہ کسی معتمد حاجی کے سپرد کر دے جو حج کے راستہ میں اس پر خرچ کرتا جائے تا کہ سفیہ اس نفقہ کو بے راہ تلف نہ کر ڈالے۔
تشريح ... قوله وان تزوج امرأة .... الخ - اگر سفیہ مجور نے کسی عورت سے نکاح کر لیا تو ہمارے اور امام احمد کے نزدیک اس کا نکاح جائز ہے۔ امام مالک، امام شافعی اور ابو الخطاب خیلی کے نزدیک اجازت ولی کے بغیر جائز نہیں کیونکہ شراء کی طرح نکاح بھی عقد معاوضہ ہے تو اجازت ولی کے بغیر جائز نہ ہوگا۔ ہم یہ کہتے ہیں کہ نکاح میں ہنرل موثر نہیں ہوتا تو حجر بھی موثر نہ ہوگا۔ نیز اسلئے بھی کہ نکاح اس کی اصلی ضروریات میں سے ہے۔
قوله وكذا اذا تزوج باربع ... الخ اسی طرح اگر سفیہ مجور نے بعقد واحد چار عورتوں سے نکاح کیا۔ یا ہر روز ایک عورت سے نکاح کرتا رہا بایں طور کہ آج ایک عورت سے مہر پر نکاح کیا اور اس کو طلاق دے کر دوسرے روز دوسری عورت سے نکاح کر لیا۔ اسی طرح کئی بار کیا۔ تب بھی نکاح جائز ہے اور مہر کا بیان بقدر مہر مثل صحیح ہوگا اور زائد مقدار باطل ہوگی ۔ وجہ وہی ہے کہ نکاح اس کی ضروریات میں
سے ہے۔
فائده ... امام ابو حنیفہ نے اسی مسئلہ سے احتجاج کیا ہے کہ سفیہ پر حجر کرنا بے سود ہے۔ کیونکہ اس سے اتلاف مال کا انسداد نہیں ہوتا ۔
He said: If he marries a woman, his marriage is valid, because jest does not affect it, and because it is from his primary needs. If he specifies a mahr for her, from him is valid the amount of her mahru'l-mithl, because it is from the necessities of marriage, and the excess is void because there is no necessity in it, and it is an undertaking by specification and he has no discretion in it, so the increase is not valid; thus he becomes like a person terminally ill. If he divorces her before consummation, half of the mahru'l-mithl is obligatory for her in his wealth, because the specification is valid up to the amount of mahru'l-mithl. Similarly, if he marries four women or one every day, due to what we discussed. He said: Zakat is taken out from the safih's wealth because it is obligatory upon him. He spends on his children, his wife, and those whose nafaqah is obligatory upon him from his relatives, because preserving his children and wife is from his needs, and spending on the relative is obligatory upon him as a right of kinship. Foolishness does not nullify people's rights. However, the judge gives the amount of zakat to him to spend it in its due channel, because his intention is necessary due to it being worship, but he sends a trustworthy person with him so he does not spend it in other ways. For nafaqah, he gives it to his trustee to spend it, because it is not worship so intention is not needed. This is contrary to if he swears an oath, makes a nadhr, or engages in zihar: then property is not binding on him; rather he expiates his oath and zihar by fasting, because it is something obligatory by his action. If we open this door, he will waste his wealth in this way, not so for that which is obligatory initially without his action. He said: If he intends Hajj al-Islam, he is not prevented from it, because it is obligatory upon him by Allah's command without his action. The judge does not hand over the nafaqah to her [for Hajj?], but gives it to a trustworthy Hajji to spend on him during the Hajj journey so he does not waste it in other ways. Translation: If an interdicted safih marries a woman, his marriage is valid because jest does not affect it, and because marriage is from his primary needs. If he specifies a mahr, the amount of mahru'l-mithl is valid, and the excess is void because there is no necessity in it, and he has undertaken it by specification while there is no benefit in it, so the increase is not valid, thus he becomes like a terminally ill person. If he divorces her before consummation, half of mahru'l-mithl is obligatory for the woman from the safih's wealth because the specification is valid up to mahru'l-mithl. Similarly, if he marries four women or one every day, due to the mentioned evidence. Zakat is taken out from the safih's wealth because it is obligatory upon him. He spends on his children, wife, and those whose nafaqah is obligatory upon him from his relatives, because preserving his children and wife is from his needs, and spending on relatives is obligatory as a right of kinship. Foolishness does not nullify people's rights. But the judge gives the amount of zakat to him to spend in its due channel because his intention is necessary due to it being worship, but he sends a trustee with him so he does not spend it improperly. For nafaqah, he gives it to his trustee to spend because it is not worship, so intention is not needed. This is contrary to if he swears an oath, makes a nadhr, or does zihar: then property is not obligatory on him; rather he expiates his oath and zihar by fasting, because it is obligatory by his action. If we open this door, he will waste his wealth in this way; not so for what is obligatory initially without his action. If the safih intends Hajj al-Islam, he is not prevented because it is obligatory upon him by Allah's command without his action. The judge does not give the nafaqah to him but entrusts it to a trustworthy Hajji to spend on him during the journey so that the safih does not waste it. Commentary ... His statement: 'If he marries a woman...' According to us and Imam Ahmad, his marriage is valid. According to Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, and Abu al-Khattab al-Khalili, it is not valid without the permission of a guardian, because marriage is a compensatory contract like purchase, so it is not valid without guardian's permission. We say: Jest does not affect marriage, so interdiction also does not affect it. Also because marriage is from his primary needs. His statement: 'Similarly if he marries four...' Similarly, if the interdicted safih marries four women in one contract, or marries one woman each day, such that today he marries one woman with a mahr, divorces her, and the next day marries another, and so on many times, still the marriage is valid and the mahr specification up to mahru'l-mithl is valid, and the excess is void. The reason is that marriage is from his needs. Benefit: Imam Abu Hanifa argued from this issue that interdiction on a safih is useless, because it does not prevent wastage of wealth.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling on the marriage of a safih (a foolish person under interdiction). It begins with the ruling statement that such a marriage is valid because marriage is not affected by jest and because it is a primary need. The mahr specified is valid only up to the amount of mahru'l-mithl (the customary mahr for a woman of her status); any excess is void because the safih has no discretion in superfluous matters. This is analogized to a terminally ill patient who cannot give away property beyond one-third. If he divorces before consummation, half of mahru'l-mithl is obligatory. This applies even if he marries four women in one contract or one each day. The text then discusses zakat and nafaqah: zakat must be paid because it is a right of Allah; the judge gives the zakat amount to the safih for him to pay with intention, but with a trustee to prevent misuse. Nafaqah for dependents is given to a trustee directly because it is not worship requiring intention. The text contrasts voluntary obligations like oaths, nadhr, and zihar, where the safih is not required to pay money but can expiate with fasting, because these arise from his own actions and allowing money payment would lead to waste. Finally, Hajj is obligatory but the judge entrusts the expenses to a reliable person. The commentary (tashrih) includes an inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafis and Ahmad bin Hanbal consider the marriage valid without a guardian, while Malik, Shafi'i, and Abu al-Khattab require a guardian's permission, arguing that marriage is a compensatory contract like purchase. The Hanafi response is that jest does not affect marriage (so interdiction based on foolishness also doesn't), and marriage is a primary need. The passage ends with a benefit (fa'ida) quoting Imam Abu Hanifa that interdiction is futile because it doesn't prevent wealth wastage. This lesson covers the complete legal unit of the safih's marriage and its related consequences.

Key Terms
زكاة (zakat): Obligatory alms on wealth reaching nisab, an act of worship.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance; obligatory spending on dependents (wife, children, needy relatives).
حج الإسلام (Hajj al-Islam): The obligatory pilgrimage to Mecca for those who are able.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the judge give nafaqah to a trustee rather than to the safih himself?
A) Because nafaqah is an act of worship requiring intention
B) Because nafaqah is not an act of worship and the safih might waste it
C) Because the safih cannot have any money
D) Because nafaqah is not obligatory

Key Takeaway
A safih must fulfill zakat, nafaqah, and hajj with judicial oversight, but expiates oaths and similar by fasting to prevent waste of wealth.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Marriage of a Safih: Validity without Guardian
Hanafi ruling on marriage of a safih with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on marriage of a safih under interdiction, explain the opposing view, and evaluate the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Marriage is a primary need, so interdiction does not block it—the safih can marry without a guardian.

A safih (foolish spendthrift) is legally interdicted (hajr) from wasting wealth. But does that interdiction extend to marriage? The Hanafi school, drawing on the principle that marriage is a primary necessity, rules that the safih's marriage is valid even without a guardian's permission. This lesson unpacks the inter-madhhab dispute, the proofs on both sides, and a subtle benefit: Imam Abu Hanifa argued that interdiction on a safih is actually counterproductive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being discussed and what the Hanafi school rules. The act is the marriage contract (nikah) performed by a safih under interdiction, and the ruling is that it is valid (sahih).
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi position (along with Imam Ahmad) holds the marriage valid. The proof: marriage is a primary necessity (darurah asliyyah) and 'low status' (al-hannar) does not affect marriage, so interdiction based on low status should not block it.
The term 'al-hannar' refers to low social standing or foolishness that triggers interdiction.
3
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, and Abu al-Khattab (Hanbali) require guardian's permission for the safih's marriage. Their proof: marriage is a contract of exchange (mu'awadah) like purchase, which requires guardian approval for the interdicted person.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafi response distinguishes marriage from pure exchange contracts: marriage is also an act of worship and a primary need. Additionally, al-hannar does not affect marriage itself, so hajr (based on hannar) cannot block it.
5
Determine the practical consequence and additional benefit
The thamarah al-khilaf: In Hanafi fiqh, the safih's marriage is valid; in other schools, it is invalid or suspended on guardian's permission. As a benefit (fa'idah), Imam Abu Hanifa argued that interdiction on a safih is futile because it encourages more harmful waste through serial marriages, as shown in the hadith cursing frequent marriage and divorce.

اسلئے کہ جب وہ بطریق بیچ و ہیرا تلاف مال سے عاجز ہو گا تو وہ بیان مہر کے ساتھ ہر روز نئی شادی کر کے مال برباد کر دے گا۔ حالانکہ اتلاف بال کا یہ طریقہ بیع و ہبہ کے طریق سے اتلاف کی بہ نسبت زیادہ مضر ہے۔ اسلئے کہ ہبہ کے طور پر خرچ کرنے سے اس کو کم از کم بر واحسان کی نیک نامی حاصل ہوگی اور تزویج مذکور کی صورت میں وہ مذمت کا مستحق ہوگا ۔ لقوله عليه السلام لعن الله كل ذواق مطلاق
His statement: 'And if he marries a woman...' etc. - If a safih under interdiction marries a woman, then according to us and Imam Ahmad, his marriage is valid. According to Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, and Abu al-Khattab al-Khalili, it is not valid without the guardian's permission, because marriage is a contract of exchange like purchase, so it is not valid without guardian's permission. We say that lowliness does not affect marriage, so interdiction also does not affect it. Also because marriage is among his primary necessities. His statement: 'And likewise if he marries four...' etc. Similarly, if a safih under interdiction marries four women in one contract, or marries one woman each day, e.g., today marries a woman with a dower, divorces her, and the next day marries another woman, and so on multiple times, then also the marriage is valid, and the specification of dower is valid up to the like dower (mahr al-mithl), and the excess amount is void. The reason is the same: marriage is among his necessities. Benefit: Imam Abu Hanifa argued from this issue that interdiction on a safih is futile, because it does not prevent waste of wealth. Because when he becomes unable to waste wealth through sale and gift, he will waste his wealth by marrying a new woman every day with a specified dower. And this method of waste is more harmful than waste through sale and gift, because through gift he at least gains a good name of charity and kindness, while in the mentioned marriages he deserves blame. Due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'May Allah curse every frequent marrier and divorcer.'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the marriage of a safih (foolish spendthrift) who is under interdiction (hajr). The Hanafi school (along with Imam Ahmad) holds that such a marriage is valid, while the Maliki, Shafi'i, and the Hanbali scholar Abu al-Khattab hold that it is invalid without a guardian's permission. The opponent analogy: marriage is a contract of exchange (like purchase), so guardian's permission is needed. The Hanafi response: marriage is different; 'al-hannar' (low social status/negligence) does not affect the validity of marriage, and moreover, marriage is a primary necessity (darurah asliyyah) for the safih, just like food and clothing. Therefore, interdiction does not block it. The same ruling extends to multiple marriages: even if he marries four wives at once or marries serially every day, each marriage is valid, but any excess dower beyond mahr al-mithl is void. The fa'idah (benefit) notes that Imam Abu Hanifa used this rule to argue that interdiction on a safih is actually counterproductive: if you block sale and gift, the safih will simply waste money through serial marriages, which is worse. A hadith is cited: the Prophet cursed 'every frequent marrier and divorcer' (dhawwaq millaq), indicating the blameworthiness of such behavior, but legally the marriage is valid.

Key Terms
سفیہ (safih): A person who is foolish and wasteful with money, such that an interdiction (hajr) is placed on his financial dealings.
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction preventing a person from freely disposing of his wealth due to foolishness (safah) or other reasons.
مہر مثل (mahr al-mithl): The dower that is appropriate for a woman of her status (like her female relatives) when no dower is specified or the specified dower is excessive.
ھنرل (al-hannar): Low social status or negligence; the quality that triggers interdiction for safah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the marriage of a safih under interdiction when he marries without a guardian's permission?
A) The marriage is invalid because the safih lacks capacity.
B) The marriage is valid only if the dower equals mahr al-mithl.
C) The marriage is valid, but the dower beyond mahr al-mithl is void.
D) The marriage is suspended until the guardian ratifies it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the marriage of a safih without a guardian because marriage is a primary need, but limits the dower to mahr al-mithl to prevent waste.

Can a Ma'dhun Slave Marry? The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling that a ma'dhun slave cannot marry himself or his slaves, with Abu Yusuf's exception for a slave-girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi ruling on a ma'dhun's authority to marry and explain Abu Yusuf's exception for a female slave.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun can only trade; marriage is not trade, so he cannot marry himself or his slaves.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a key limitation of a ma'dhun slave's permission. While the ma'dhun is authorized to trade within the limits of his permit, does that authorization extend to marriage? The text presents the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) that it does not, and records Abu Yusuf's dissenting opinion concerning a female slave. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi boundaries of trade vs. non-trade acts.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify the general ruling: the ma'dhun cannot marry himself or any of his slaves, because permission covers trade only, and marriage is not trade.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's exception
Note that Abu Yusuf allows the ma'dhun to marry off his female slave (amah) because it resembles hiring out (ijarah) – obtaining wealth through her usufruct.
3
Follow Abu Yusuf's proof
Trace his qiyas: marrying off a female slave is a means of acquiring wealth (dower) in return for her usufruct, just as ijarah yields rent. Hence it falls under trade.
4
Analyze the majority's response
Understand the counter: marriage involves rights (milk al-nikah) that go beyond mere usufruct; it is fundamentally a different contract, not trade. Permission is limited strictly to trade.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and analogies
Under Abu Yusuf's view, the ma'dhun can arrange the marriage and receive the dower. The same ikhtilaf applies to other limited agents: the permitted minor, mudarib, 'inan partner, father, and executor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وليس له ان يتزوج لانه ليس بتجارة ولا يزوج مماليكه وقال ابويوسف يزوج الامة لانه تحصيل المال بمنافعها فاشبه اجارتها ولهما ان الاذن يتضمن التجارة وهذا ليس بتجارة ولهذا لا يملك تزويج العبد وعلى هذا الخلاف الصبي الماذون والمضارب والشريك شركة عنان والاب والوصى ترجمہ اور مازون کو نکاح کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ یہ تجارت نہیں ہے اور اپنے مملوکوں کا نکاح بھی نہیں کر سکتا ۔ امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ باندی کا نکاح کر سکتا ہے کیونکہ یہ اس کے منافع کے عوض مال حاصل کرنا ہے تو اس کو مزدوری پر دینے کی طرح ہو گیا۔ طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اجازت تجارت کو شامل ہے اور یہ تجارت نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے وہ غلام کا نکاح کرنے کا مالک نہیں ہوتا۔ اور اسی اختلاف پر سے صبى ماذون ، مضارب ، شرکت عنان کا شریک ، باپ اور وصی ۔
He said: And it is not for him to marry because it is not trade, nor may he marry off his slaves. Abu Yusuf said: He may marry off the female slave (amah) because it is acquiring wealth through her usufruct, so it resembles hiring her out. (And the two said [i.e., Abu Hanifa and Muhammad]: Permission includes trade, and this is not trade, so he does not own the authority to marry off a slave. And upon this difference are the permitted minor, the mudarib, the partner in an 'inan partnership, the father, and the executor.) Translation: And the ma'dhun does not have the authority to marry because it is not trade, nor can he marry off his slaves. Imam Abu Yusuf says he can marry off the female slave because it is obtaining wealth through her usufruct, so it becomes like hiring her out. The two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad] argue that permission encompasses trade, and this is not trade, so he does not have the authority to marry off a slave. And upon this difference are the permitted minor, the mudarib, the partner in an 'inan partnership, the father, and the executor.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ma'dhun's authority to marry. The matn states a clear ruling: a ma'dhun slave cannot marry himself, nor can he marry off his slaves, because marriage is not trade—the permission (idhn) given to the ma'dhun is only for trade. Then Imam Abu Yusuf's exceptional view is recorded: he allows the ma'dhun to marry off his female slave (amah) because doing so involves obtaining wealth (the dower) in exchange for her usufruct, similar to hiring her out. 'Lahuma' (the two) refers to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, who maintain that marriage is not trade, so it is outside the scope of permission. They argue that since idhn covers only trade, and marriage is not trade, the ma'dhun cannot perform any marriage-related acts. The same ikhtilaf applies analogously to other legal persons with limited permission: the permitted minor (sabi ma'dhun), the mudarib (sleeping partner), the partner in an 'inan partnership, the father (in his capacity as guardian of his children's property), and the executor (wasi) of a will. This dispute highlights the Hanafi distinction between trade and other property-related acts.

Key Terms
تزويج (tazwij): The act of giving in marriage; marrying off someone.
أمة (amah): A female slave owned by the ma'dhun.
مضارب (mudarib): A sleeping partner who provides labor in a mudarabah partnership.
شريك عنان (sharik 'inan): A partner in an 'inan partnership where both contribute capital and labor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the majority Hanafi view), why may a ma'dhun slave not marry off his male slave?
A) Because the male slave might object.
B) Because marriage is not trade, and permission is only for trade.
C) Because the male slave cannot be sold.
D) Because the ma'dhun himself cannot marry.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun's permission is strictly limited to trade; any marriage contract he performs is void because marriage is not a trade transaction in Hanafi fiqh.

Marriage by a Ma'dhun Slave: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ma'dhun's marriage capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position on a ma'dhun slave's capacity to marry himself and his slaves, explain Abu Yusuf's dissenting view on marrying the female slave, and identify the proofs (dalil) for each position.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun cannot marry himself or his slaves—marriage is not trade, even when it yields wealth.

This passage addresses a key limitation on the permission granted to a ma'dhun slave to trade. While he may buy and sell, can he contract marriage—for himself or for his slaves? The Hanafi school is divided: the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) strictly limit the permission to trade acts, while Abu Yusuf extends it to marriage of a female slave due to the financial benefit (mahr). Let us examine the reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Identify the ruling from the text: the ma'dhun cannot marry himself or his male/female slaves because marriage is not trade.
The ruling is from the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note that Abu Yusuf dissents regarding the female slave: he allows marrying her because it brings mahr, which is wealth from benefits, resembling hiring.
Abu Yusuf does not allow marrying the male slave.
3
Compare the proofs
For the majority: permission (idhn) includes only trade acts; marriage is not trade. For Abu Yusuf: marriage of a female slave is a means of acquiring wealth (mahr), like hiring, which is trade.
The key distinction is whether the act is categorized as trade.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If a ma'dhun marries his female slave, according to Abu Yusuf it is valid and the mahr goes to trading stock; according to the majority it is invalid and the ma'dhun may be liable for usurpation.
This practical difference affects the validity and financial consequences.
5
Note the extension to other parties
The same disagreement applies to the ma'dhun minor, mudarib, partner in shirkat al-'inan, father, and executor. All agree that the male slave cannot be married.
This extension shows the principle's scope.

He said: 'And he does not have the right to marry, because it is not trade, nor can he marry his slaves.' Abu Yusuf said: 'He can marry the female slave, because it is acquisition of wealth from her benefits, so it resembles hiring her.' The two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad] argued: 'Permission includes trade, and this is not trade, therefore he does not have the right to marry the male slave.' The same disagreement applies to the ma'dhun minor, the mudarib, the partner in shirkat al-'inan, the father, and the executor. Translation: And the ma'dhun does not have the authority to contract his own marriage because it is not trade, nor can he marry his male or female slaves. Imam Abu Yusuf says he can marry the female slave because it is acquiring wealth through her benefits, so it resembles hiring her out. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) argue that permission encompasses trade, and this is not trade, so he does not have the right to marry any slave. The same disagreement applies to the ma'dhun minor, mudarib, partner in shirkat al-'inan, father, and executor. Explanation of his statement 'and he does not have the right to marry...' The ma'dhun slave does not have the authority to marry himself because it is not trade. Also, according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and the three Imams, he cannot marry any male or female slave in his trading stock. However, according to Imam Abu Yusuf, he has the authority to marry the female slave because wealth (mahr) is obtained from her benefits, and the purpose of permission is to acquire wealth, so it is like hiring her out, which is permissible. The evidence of the two is that the purpose of permission is acquisition of wealth, but not absolutely; he must acquire wealth through trade, and marrying a female slave is not trade, so he is not authorized to marry any slave. He said: 'And on this disagreement...' The same difference applies to the ma'dhun minor, mudarib, partner in shirkat al-'inan, father, and executor: according to the two, they cannot marry the female slave, but according to Abu Yusuf, they can. There is agreement that they cannot marry the male slave. The meaning is that if a minor owns a female slave, his father does not have the authority to marry her off; and if the father died, the executor also does not have that authority. Similarly, if someone is given capital for mudarabah, the mudarib cannot marry the female slave of the mudarabah capital; likewise, the partner in shirkat al-'inan and the ma'dhun minor cannot. Note: Shaykh Sighnaqi objected to this statement, because earlier the author of Hidayah wrote in the chapter on Kitabat al-Mukatib, under 'What is permissible for the mukatib to do' (page 143): 'Similarly, the father and executor regarding the slave of a minor are in the position of the mukatib.' Meaning, the father and executor have the authority to marry the minor's female slave, because they are deemed to be like a mukatib without dispute, and a mukatib has the authority to marry his female slave by consensus. But here he says 'on this disagreement...the father and executor.' Then the said scholar (Shaykh Sighnaqi) stated that the narration from Kitab al-Mukatib is more correct, because it agrees with al-Mabsut, Mukhtasar al-Kafi, and Ahkam al-Sighar. Accordingly, in al-Mabsut's chapter on marriage of slaves: 'If the father marries his minor son's female slave, it is valid; likewise, if the executor marries the orphan's female slave; likewise, if the mukatib marries his female slave; likewise, if the partner in muwada'ah (or mufawadah) marries a female slave from the partnership, because marrying a female slave is from the contracts of acquisition—through it, mahr is acquired and her maintenance is removed from the owner, and these four have the right of acquisition.'
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage addresses the capacity of a ma'dhun slave (and analogous parties) to contract marriage—both for himself and for his slaves. The base text (from al-Hidayah) rules that a ma'dhun cannot marry himself because marriage is not trade, nor can he marry his male or female slaves. Abu Yusuf disagrees regarding the female slave: since marriage of a female slave brings mahr (dower), which is wealth acquired from her benefits, it resembles hiring her out, which is permissible. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) respond that permission to trade is limited to trade acts; marriage is not a trade act, even if it brings wealth, because it is not categorized as trade; thus a ma'dhun cannot marry any slave. The same difference is extended to: a ma'dhun minor (a minor given permission to trade), a mudarib, a partner in shirkat al-'inan, a father managing a minor's property, and an executor of a will. According to Abu Yusuf, all these can marry the female slave under their control because it is a means of acquisition; according to the majority, they cannot. All agree that the male slave cannot be married off by these parties. The passage then includes a significant note (tanbih) from Shaykh Sighnaqi, who points out an apparent contradiction: earlier in the chapter on Kitabat al-Mukatib, the author of al-Hidayah stated that the father and executor can marry the minor's female slave, likening them to a mukatib. However, here (in the chapter on Ma'dhun) the author says they are subject to the same disagreement. Shaykh Sighnaqi resolves this by stating that the earlier narration (from Kitab al-Mukatib) is more correct, supported by al-Mabsut and other authorities. He then quotes al-Mabsut allowing the father, executor, mukatib, and mufawid partner to marry the female slave, because it is a contract of acquisition (min 'uqud al-iktisab) that brings mahr and removes maintenance. The implication is that the correct Hanafi position is that the father, executor, mukatib, and mufawid partner can marry the female slave, while the ma'dhun and mudarib remain subject to the dispute. The passage thus provides a nuanced intra-Hanafi discussion with a preferred resolution aligning with al-Mabsut.

Key Terms
مملوک (mamlūk): Slave owned by the ma'dhun; can be male or female.
ترویج (tarvīj): Marrying off (a female slave); literally 'promotion'.
عقود اکتساب (‘uqūd al-iktisāb): Contracts of acquisition, i.e., transactions that bring profit, such as a marriage of a female slave (according to the supporting view) or hiring.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), why can a ma'dhun slave not marry his female slave?
A) Because the female slave belongs to the master, not the ma'dhun.
B) Because marriage is not a trade act, and permission to trade does not include non-trade acts.
C) Because the ma'dhun lacks the capacity to consent.
D) Because the mahr would go to the master.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave cannot marry himself or his slaves because marriage is not a trade act, though Abu Yusuf dissents regarding the female slave, with the same disagreement applying to other fiduciaries.

Resolving the Contradiction: The Authoritative View on Marrying Female Slaves
Tanbih resolving an apparent contradiction in al-Hidayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Shaykh Sighnaqi's resolution of the apparent contradiction between the Hidayah's chapters and apply the preferred view that father, executor, mukatib, and mufawid may marry the female slave.

Core Idea
Father, executor, mukatib, and mufawid can marry the female slave—al-Mabsut corrects the earlier restriction.

This passage contains a critical note (tanbih) from Shaykh Sighnaqi, who identifies an apparent contradiction in al-Hidayah: earlier, in the chapter on the mukatib, the author allowed the father and executor to marry the minor's female slave, but here he says they are subject to the same disagreement. Shaykh Sighnaqi resolves this by preferring the earlier narration from al-Mabsut and other authorities, establishing the correct position. Let us examine the textual analysis and the resulting ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
In the chapter on Kitabat al-Mukatib, al-Hidayah stated that the father and executor are like the mukatib and can marry the minor's female slave. Here, in the chapter on the ma'dhun, the author says they are subject to the same disagreement (i.e., the majority view prohibits).
This suggests two conflicting rulings.
2
Understand Shaykh Sighnaqi's objection
He points out the contradiction and asserts that the earlier narration (from Kitab al-Mukatib) is more correct because it is supported by al-Mabsut, Mukhtasar al-Kafi, and Ahkam al-Sighar.
He uses cross-referencing to resolve the conflict.
3
Examine the supporting text from al-Mabsut
Al-Mabsut explicitly allows the father, executor, mukatib, and mufawid to marry the female slave, because marrying a female slave is a contract of acquisition (min 'uqud al-iktisab) that brings mahr and removes maintenance.
This provides the legal basis.
4
Apply the preferred view
The correct Hanafi position is that these four parties (father, executor, mukatib, mufawid) can marry the female slave, while the ma'dhun and mudarib remain subject to the original dispute.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

He said: 'And he does not have the right to marry, because it is not trade, nor can he marry his slaves.' Abu Yusuf said: 'He can marry the female slave, because it is acquisition of wealth from her benefits, so it resembles hiring her.' The two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad] argued: 'Permission includes trade, and this is not trade, therefore he does not have the right to marry the male slave.' The same disagreement applies to the ma'dhun minor, the mudarib, the partner in shirkat al-'inan, the father, and the executor. Translation: And the ma'dhun does not have the authority to contract his own marriage because it is not trade, nor can he marry his male or female slaves. Imam Abu Yusuf says he can marry the female slave because it is acquiring wealth through her benefits, so it resembles hiring her out. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) argue that permission encompasses trade, and this is not trade, so he does not have the right to marry any slave. The same disagreement applies to the ma'dhun minor, mudarib, partner in shirkat al-'inan, father, and executor. Explanation of his statement 'and he does not have the right to marry...' The ma'dhun slave does not have the authority to marry himself because it is not trade. Also, according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and the three Imams, he cannot marry any male or female slave in his trading stock. However, according to Imam Abu Yusuf, he has the authority to marry the female slave because wealth (mahr) is obtained from her benefits, and the purpose of permission is to acquire wealth, so it is like hiring her out, which is permissible. The evidence of the two is that the purpose of permission is acquisition of wealth, but not absolutely; he must acquire wealth through trade, and marrying a female slave is not trade, so he is not authorized to marry any slave. He said: 'And on this disagreement...' The same difference applies to the ma'dhun minor, mudarib, partner in shirkat al-'inan, father, and executor: according to the two, they cannot marry the female slave, but according to Abu Yusuf, they can. There is agreement that they cannot marry the male slave. The meaning is that if a minor owns a female slave, his father does not have the authority to marry her off; and if the father died, the executor also does not have that authority. Similarly, if someone is given capital for mudarabah, the mudarib cannot marry the female slave of the mudarabah capital; likewise, the partner in shirkat al-'inan and the ma'dhun minor cannot. Note: Shaykh Sighnaqi objected to this statement, because earlier the author of Hidayah wrote in the chapter on Kitabat al-Mukatib, under 'What is permissible for the mukatib to do' (page 143): 'Similarly, the father and executor regarding the slave of a minor are in the position of the mukatib.' Meaning, the father and executor have the authority to marry the minor's female slave, because they are deemed to be like a mukatib without dispute, and a mukatib has the authority to marry his female slave by consensus. But here he says 'on this disagreement...the father and executor.' Then the said scholar (Shaykh Sighnaqi) stated that the narration from Kitab al-Mukatib is more correct, because it agrees with al-Mabsut, Mukhtasar al-Kafi, and Ahkam al-Sighar. Accordingly, in al-Mabsut's chapter on marriage of slaves: 'If the father marries his minor son's female slave, it is valid; likewise, if the executor marries the orphan's female slave; likewise, if the mukatib marries his female slave; likewise, if the partner in muwada'ah (or mufawadah) marries a female slave from the partnership, because marrying a female slave is from the contracts of acquisition—through it, mahr is acquired and her maintenance is removed from the owner, and these four have the right of acquisition.'
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage addresses the capacity of a ma'dhun slave (and analogous parties) to contract marriage—both for himself and for his slaves. The base text (from al-Hidayah) rules that a ma'dhun cannot marry himself because marriage is not trade, nor can he marry his male or female slaves. Abu Yusuf disagrees regarding the female slave: since marriage of a female slave brings mahr (dower), which is wealth acquired from her benefits, it resembles hiring her out, which is permissible. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) respond that permission to trade is limited to trade acts; marriage is not a trade act, even if it brings wealth, because it is not categorized as trade; thus a ma'dhun cannot marry any slave. The same difference is extended to: a ma'dhun minor (a minor given permission to trade), a mudarib, a partner in shirkat al-'inan, a father managing a minor's property, and an executor of a will. According to Abu Yusuf, all these can marry the female slave under their control because it is a means of acquisition; according to the majority, they cannot. All agree that the male slave cannot be married off by these parties. The passage then includes a significant note (tanbih) from Shaykh Sighnaqi, who points out an apparent contradiction: earlier in the chapter on Kitabat al-Mukatib, the author of al-Hidayah stated that the father and executor can marry the minor's female slave, likening them to a mukatib. However, here (in the chapter on Ma'dhun) the author says they are subject to the same disagreement. Shaykh Sighnaqi resolves this by stating that the earlier narration (from Kitab al-Mukatib) is more correct, supported by al-Mabsut and other authorities. He then quotes al-Mabsut allowing the father, executor, mukatib, and mufawid partner to marry the female slave, because it is a contract of acquisition (min 'uqud al-iktisab) that brings mahr and removes maintenance. The implication is that the correct Hanafi position is that the father, executor, mukatib, and mufawid partner can marry the female slave, while the ma'dhun and mudarib remain subject to the dispute. The passage thus provides a nuanced intra-Hanafi discussion with a preferred resolution aligning with al-Mabsut.

Key Terms
مکاتب (mukātib): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn and purchase his freedom.
مفاوض (mufāwiḍ): A partner in a full partnership (mufawadah) where each partner is an agent and surety for the other.
تنبیہ (tanbīh): A critical note pointing out an issue or refinement in the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The narration from Kitab al-Mukatib that they can, supported by al-Mabsut.
C) Both are equally valid.
D) The matter is undecided.

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi view is that father, executor, mukatib, and mufawid can marry the female slave, as it is a contract of acquisition, resolving an apparent contradiction in al-Hidayah.
