Effect of Master's Death, Insanity, or Apostasy on Ma'dhun Slave
Ruling: ma'dhun slave becomes restricted upon master's death, insanity, or apostasy with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three events that revoke a ma'dhun slave's permission to trade and explain the Hanafi legal reasoning (qiyas) that permission, being non-binding, requires the master's capacity for both its inception and continuance.

Core Idea
Permission not binding: continuation requires same capacity as initiation.

In Hanafi fiqh, a ma'dhun slave is a slave permitted by his master to engage in trade. This permission is not an irrevocable contract; it can be rescinded. This lesson explores three specific events that automatically revoke the permission: the master's death, permanent insanity, or apostasy with flight to enemy territory. The legal reasoning is based on a key principle: the continuation of a non-binding permission has the same legal requirements as its initiation. Understanding this reveals the internal logic of Hanafi rulings on legal capacity (ahliyyah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal event and its result
What specific events cause the ma'dhun to become mahjur? From the passage, death, insanity, and apostasy joining Dar al-Harb. The result is that the permission ends automatically.
2
Understand the nature of permission (idhn)
The passage states that permission (idhn) is ghayr lazim (non-binding). This means it can be revoked at will. This is a key Hanafi principle.
What does 'non-binding' mean for the slave's trade activities?
3
Apply the principle of continuity equal to initiation
The proof states that for a non-binding disposition, its continuation has the same ruling as its initiation. So if permission requires the master's capacity at the start, it also requires it throughout. Death and insanity destroy capacity.
This is a rational analogical reasoning (qiyas).
4
Treat joining Dar al-Harb as legal death
The passage explains that the apostate who joins Dar al-Harb is considered legally dead (mawt hukman) because his property is divided among heirs. Hence the same consequence.
This shows how Hanafi jurists extend rulings based on effective cause (illah).
5
Determine the practical scope
The commentary highlights that the slave becomes mahjur regardless of whether he knows of the event. Knowledge is not a condition.
Why might someone think knowledge is needed? Possibly because the slave needs to act on the revocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مولیٰ فوت ہو گیا یا مجنون یا مرتد ہو کر دار الحرب چلا گیا تو ماذون محجور ہو جائے گا
قال ولومات المولى او جن او لحق بدار الحرب مرتدا صار الماذون محجورا عليه لان الاذن غير لازم وما لا يكون لازما من التصرف يعطى لدوامه حكم الابتداء هذا هو الاصل فلابد من قيام اهلية الاذن في حالة البقاء وهى تنعدم بالموت والجنون وكذا باللحوق لانه موت حكما حتى يقسم ماله بين ورثته
ترجمہ اگر آقا مر گیا یا دیوانہ ہو گیا یا مرتد ہو کر دار الحرب میں مل گیا تو ماذون غلام محجور ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اجازت غیر لازمی ہے اور جو تصرف لازم نہ ہو اس کے دوام کو ابتداء کا حکم دیا جاتا ہے۔ یہی اصل ہے۔ پس حالت بقاء میں بھی اہلیت اذن کا ہونا ضروری ہے۔ حالانکہ وہ موت اور جنون سے معدوم ہو جاتی ہے اور ایسے ہی دار الحرب میں مل جانے سے بھی کیونکہ لحوق حکمی موت ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ اس کا مال اس کے ورثہ میں تقسیم کر دیا جاتا ہے۔
تشریح قوله و لو مات.... الخ اگر عبد ماذون کا مالک مر جائے یا دائگی مجنون ہو جائے یا مرتد ہو کر دار الحرب چلا جائے تب بھی عبد ماذون محجور ہو جائے گا۔ خواہ غلام کو ان امور کا علم ہو گیا ہو یا نہ ہو۔ وجہ یہ ہے کہ اذن غیر لازم تصرف ہے تو اس کی بقاء کا بھی وہی حکم ہوگا جو ابتداء کا ہے۔ تو جس طرح ابتداء اہلیت اذن کا ہونا ضروری ہے اسی طرح بقاء بھی اس کا ہونا ضروری ہے۔ حالانکہ موت اور جنون کی وجہ سے اہلیت اذن معدوم ہو گئی۔ نیز دارالحرب میں مل جانے سے بھی لیاقت جاتی رہی کیونکہ یہ بھی حکمی موت ہے حتی کہ اس کا مال اسکے وارثوں کو بانٹ دیا جاتا ہے لہذا غلام محجور ہو جائے گا۔
If the master dies, or becomes insane, or apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb, then the ma'dhun becomes mahjur (restricted). He (the author) said: If the master dies, or becomes insane, or joins Dar al-Harb as an apostate, the ma'dhun becomes mahjur upon him because permission (idhn) is not binding. And what is not binding from dispositions is given, for its continuation, the ruling of initiation. This is the principle. So the capacity of permission must exist in the state of continuance, and it is annulled by death and insanity, and similarly by joining (Dar al-Harb) because it is legal death, to the extent that his wealth is divided among his heirs. Translation: If the master dies, or becomes insane, or joins Dar al-Harb as an apostate, then the ma'dhun slave becomes mahjur. Because permission is non-binding, and a non-binding disposition is given the ruling of initiation for its continuation. This is the principle. So in the state of continuance, the capacity for permission is necessary. And it is eliminated by death and insanity, and similarly by joining Dar al-Harb, because joining is legal death, to the extent that his wealth is divided among his heirs. Commentary on his saying "and if he dies...": If the owner of a ma'dhun slave dies, or becomes permanently insane, or apostatizes and goes to Dar al-Harb, then the ma'dhun slave becomes mahjur, whether the slave knows of these events or not. The reason is that permission is a non-binding disposition, so its continuation has the same ruling as its initiation. Just as at the beginning the capacity of permission is necessary, so too in continuance it is necessary. And death and insanity eliminate the capacity of permission. Similarly, joining Dar al-Harb eliminates it because it is legal death, to the extent that his wealth is distributed among his heirs. So the slave becomes mahjur.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage states a ruling concerning a ma'dhun slave (a slave permitted to trade). The author explains that if the master dies, becomes insane, or apostatizes and flees to enemy territory, the slave's permission (idhn) is revoked, and he becomes restricted (mahjur). The legal reasoning is that the permission is not binding (ghayr lazim); it is a revocable authorization. The rule for continuation of such a non-binding permission is the same as its inception: the master must have legal capacity (ahliyyah) at the time of both grant and continuance. Death and insanity clearly destroy capacity. Joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate is treated as legal death (mawt hukman) because the apostate's property is divided among heirs. Therefore, the permission ends. The commentary emphasizes that this applies whether or not the slave knows of the event. This is a clear example of the Hanafi principle that idhn ghayr lazim (permission is not binding) and that continuation requires the same conditions as initiation.

Key Terms
ماذون (ma'dhun): A slave whom the master has permitted to engage in trade, thus allowed to incur debts and conduct business within limits.
محجور (mahjur): A person placed under legal restriction (hajr), unable to conduct financial transactions without a guardian.
اذن (idhn): Permission granted by the master to the slave to engage in trade; a revocable authorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The master becomes insane
C) The slave runs away
D) The master gives a gift

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave's permission to trade is automatically revoked upon the master's death, permanent insanity, or apostasy with flight to Dar al-Harb, because the permission is non-binding and requires the master's capacity for its continuance.

Ma'dhun Slave Woman Becomes Restricted upon Becoming Umm Walad
Ruling on ma'dhun slave woman becoming umm walad; dispute with Imam Zufar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that a ma'dhun slave woman becomes restricted upon bearing her master's child, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
Becoming an umm walad by custom revokes the slave's permission to trade, protecting creditors' rights.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: a ma'dhun slave woman (one given permission to trade) gives birth to her master's child, making her an umm walad. The Hanafi school holds that this event automatically revokes her permission and restricts her, contrary to Imam Zufar's view that permission continues. The ruling hinges on customary indication and creditor protection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Recognize the situation: a ma'dhun slave woman (permitted to trade) gives birth to her master's child, thereby becoming an umm walad.
Umm walad status is pivotal.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi position is that she becomes restricted (mahjur) immediately; her permission is revoked.
This is a hukm wad'i.
3
Identify the opposing view
Imam Zufar holds that she remains ma'dhun, analogizing continuance to initiation.
He uses qiyas.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The master will customarily confine her after childbirth; this customary indication (dilaalah 'aadah) implies revocation. Also, liability for her value if debts exist protects creditors.
Explicit permission overrides implication initially, but not after the event.
5
Evaluate the practical difference
Under the Hanafi position, immediate restriction prevents new debts and allows creditors to claim against the master. Under Zufar's view, she continues trading until explicit revocation.
Thamarah al-khilaf: creditors' rights are affected.

قال واذا ولدت الماذون لها من مولاها فذلك حجر عليها خلافا لزفر وهو يعتبر البقاء بالابتداء ولنا ان الظاهر انه يحصنها بعد الولادة فيكون دلالة الحجر عادة بخلاف الابتداء لان الصريح قاض على الدلالة ويضمن المولى قيمتها ان ركبتها ديون لاتلافه محلا تعلق به حق الغرماء اذ به يمتنع البيع وبه يقضى حقهم
ترجمہ اگر ماذونہ باندی نے اپنے آقا سے بچہ جنا تو یہ اس پر حجر ہے۔ برخلاف امام زفر کے وہ بقاء کو ابتداء پر قیاس کرتے ہیں۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ آقا اس کو لوگوں کے میل جول سے محفوظ رکھے گا۔ پس یہ از راہ عادت حجر کی دلیل ہے۔ بخلاف ابتداء کے کیونکہ صریح اجازت دلالت پر حاکم ہوتی ہے اور ضامن ہو گا آقا اس کی قیمت کا اگر اس پر قرض چڑھ گئے ہوں اسکے ایسے محل کو تلف کر دینے کی وجہ سے جس کے ساتھ قرض خواہوں کا حق متعلق تھا۔ کیونکہ استمیلاد کی وجہ سے اس کی بیع ممتنع ہو گئی ۔ حالانکہ بیع ہی سے ان کا حق پکایا جاتا ہے۔
He said: If a ma'dhun slave woman gives birth to a child from her master, that is a restriction (hajr) upon her, contrary to Zufar. He considers continuance like initiation. Our proof is that it is apparent that he (the master) will confine her after birth, so it is a customary indication of restriction, contrary to initiation because explicit text (the permission) overrides implication. And the master is liable for her value if debts have mounted upon her, because he destroyed (her status as) a locus to which the creditors' right was attached, since due to istilad (bearing a child) her sale is prevented, whereas by sale their right is satisfied. Translation: If a ma'dhun slave woman gives birth to a child from her master, then that is a restriction upon her, contrary to Zufar. He analogizes continuance to initiation. Our proof is that it is apparent that the master will confine her (protect her) after childbirth, so it is a customary indication of restriction, unlike the initiation because explicit text overrides implication. And the master is liable for her value if debts have accrued upon her, due to his destroying a locus to which the creditors' right was attached, because istilad prevents her sale, while by sale their right is satisfied.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage deals with a ma'dhun slave woman who bears a child from her master, thereby becoming an umm walad (mother of her master's child). The Hanafi ruling is that her permission is revoked and she becomes restricted (mahjur). Imam Zufar (within Hanafi) disagrees, analogizing the continuance of permission to its initiation: if she was allowed to trade before childbirth, she should remain allowed. The Hanafi response is that after childbirth, the master will naturally confine her (protect her from public interaction), which is a customary indication (dilaalah 'urfiyyah) that the permission is no longer operative. At the initiation, explicit permission overrides any implication, but after childbirth, the implication becomes effective. Additionally, if the slave woman had debts, the master becomes liable for her value (up to the debts) because by making her an umm walad (which prevents her sale), he has destroyed the creditors' recourse to her sale for debt repayment. This shows the Hanafi concern for protecting creditors' rights.

Key Terms
ام ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child recognized as the master's, thereby gaining certain rights such as freedom upon master's death.
استمیلاد (istilad): The act of a slave woman bearing a child from her master, making her an umm walad.
دلالة عادة (dilaalah 'aadah): A legal indication based on customary practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the passage, why does a ma'dhun slave woman become restricted after bearing her master's child?
A) Because the master explicitly revokes permission
B) Because the child is a new burden
C) Because custom indicates the master will confine her, which is contrary to trading
D) Because the slave woman loses her capacity

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave woman automatically becomes restricted upon becoming umm walad, due to customary indication and to protect creditors' rights.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
12 min
Liability for Usurper's Zina with Slave-Girl Who Dies in Postpartum
Ruling on liability when usurper commits zina, impregnates, returns her, and she dies in postpartum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding liability for a usurper who commits zina with a slave-girl that dies after return, and identify the key factor (day of conception vs. valid return) that determines the ruling.

Core Idea
A valid return does not necessarily absolve the usurper if the death is caused by his unlawful act.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about financial liability when a usurper commits zina with a usurped slave-girl, impregnates her, returns her, and she later dies in postpartum. At the heart of the disagreement is whether the return resets liability or whether the causal link between the zina and the death persists. Understanding this case sharpens your grasp of how Hanafi jurists weigh causation (sabab) against subsequent events.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Read the passage and note the sequence: usurpation – zina – pregnancy – return – death during postpartum. This is the full fact pattern.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa holds the usurper liable for the value of the slave-girl on the day of conception. Identify his reasoning: the pregnancy and death are consequences of the zina.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the usurper is not liable. Their reasoning: the return was valid, and the death resulted from a new event (postpartum) not caused by the usurper.
4
Identify the Point of Dispute
The dispute hinges on whether the causal link between zina and death remains after a valid return. Abu Hanifa sees continuity; the Sahibayn see a break.
5
Apply the Dispute to Similar Cases
Consider how this principle might apply to other scenarios, e.g., usurping property and then damaging it after return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باندی غصب کی اس کے ساتھ زنا کیا سودہ حاملہ ہوگئی اسے رد کر دیا وہ نفاس میں مرگئی ، اس کے حاملہ ہونے کے دن کی قیمت کا ضمان ہوگا قال ومن غصب جارية فزنى بها فحبلت ثم ردها وماتت فى نفاسها يضمن قيمتها يوم علقت ولا ضمان عليه في الحرة هذا عند ابي حنيفة وقالا لا يضمن فى الامة ايضا لهما ان الرد قد صح والهلاك بعده بسبب حدث في
A slave-girl was usurped, he committed zina with her, she became pregnant, he returned her, and she died in postpartum. He will be liable for her value on the day she conceived. He said: 'And whoever usurps a slave-girl and commits zina with her, and she becomes pregnant, then he returns her, and she dies in her postpartum, he guarantees her value on the day she conceived, and there is no liability on the free woman. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is not liable even for the slave-girl. For them, the return was valid and the destruction after that occurred due to an event that happened in...'
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a usurper who commits zina with a usurped slave-girl, impregnates her, returns her, and then she dies during postpartum (nifas). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the usurper is liable for her value on the day she conceived (i.e., the day of the zina). The reason is that the pregnancy and subsequent death are consequences of the zina, and the return does not absolve him of liability for the damage caused by his unlawful act. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that since he returned her validly, and the death occurred due to a cause (postpartum) that happened after the return, he is not liable for the slave-girl. However, they agree that there is no liability for a free woman (if she were free, no financial liability for zina). The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument of the Sahibayn continues on the next page, stating that the death is a new event not caused by the usurper. This is a nuanced area where the nature of liability for consequential damages is debated within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
زنا (zina): Unlawful sexual intercourse.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding period after childbirth.
علق ('aliqa): To become pregnant; the day of conception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why is the usurper not liable for the slave-girl who dies in postpartum after being returned?
A) Because the usurper repented.
B) Because the return was valid and the death was a new event not caused by the usurper.
C) Because the slave-girl was not valuable.
D) Because the pregnancy was not proven.

Key Takeaway
In intra-Hanafi disputes on ghasb, the key question is whether the causal link between the wrongful act and the damage persists after a valid return; Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on this.
